Definitions of Lutuf, Tawfiq, and Isma: Views of Various Schools
Definitions of lutuf, tawfiq, and isma from various schools.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different definitions of lutuf, tawfiq, and isma given by the Ash'aris, philosophers, and Mu'tazila, and the distinction between effective and proximate grace.

Core Idea
Grace (lutf) is what guides choice; success and infallibility are its specific applications.

This lesson unpacks the core terms of divine grace, success, and infallibility as understood by different theological schools. The passage presents definitions from Ash'aris, philosophers, and Mu'tazila, showing how each group conceptualizes God's role in guiding or abandoning human beings. Understanding these definitions is essential for grasping debates on human agency and divine causation.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get an overview of the terms. Then read the translation. Notice the sequence of definitions from different groups.
The passage starts with a general statement about creating power for obedience/disobedience.
2
Identify the three main groups
Find the definitions attributed to the philosophers and the Mu'tazila. Compare their understanding of 'isma and lutf.
The philosophers define 'isma as a disposition (malaka) that prevents sin despite ability.
3
Understand the Mu'tazilite classification
The Mu'tazila distinguish between muhassil (effective) and muqarrib (proximate) grace, and assign specific terms: tawfiq for obligatory acts, 'isma for avoiding evil.
This shows how tawfiq and 'isma are subtypes of lutf.
4
Note the Mu'tazilite interpretation of divine will
The Mu'tazila argue that lutf varies by person and that God's will in verses like 'if We willed' refers to compulsion, not guidance.
This connects to broader issues of predestination.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثاني اللطف والتوفيق والعصمة خلق قدرة الطاعة والخذلان خلق قدرة المعصية فالموفق لا يعصي وبالعكس وقيل العصمة (١) أن لا يخلق الله تعالى في العبد الذنب، وقيل خاصية يمتنع بسببه صدور الذنب عنه، وقالت الفلاسفة ملكة تمنع الفجور مع القدرة عليه، وقالت المعتزلة اللطف ما يختار المكلف عنده الطاعة تركاً أو إتياناً أو يقرب منهما مع تمكنه في الحالين، ويسميان المحصل والمقرب، ويختص المحصل للواجب باسم التوفيق، وترك القبيح باسم العصمة، وقيل: التوفيق : خلق لطف يعلم الله أن العبد يطيع عنده والخذلان منع اللطف والعصمة لطف لا داعي معه إلى ترك الطاعة ولا إلى ارتكاب المعصية مع القدرة عليهما . قالوا : واللطف يختلف باختلاف المكلفين، وليس في معلومه ما هو لطف في حق الكل ومن ههنا حملوا المشيئة في مثل قوله تعالى ولو شئنا لآتينا كل نفس هداها ) (۲) على مشيئة قسر وإلجاء) . خلق قدرة الطاعة والخذلان خلق قدرة المعصية، والعصمة هي التوفيق بعينه، فإن عممت كانت توفيقاً عاماً، وإن خصصت كانت توفيقاً خاصاً كذا ذكره إمام الحرمين (۳) وقال : ثم الموفق لا يعصي إذ لا قدرة له على المعصية وبالعكس
The Second Topic: Al-Lutuf (Grace) and Al-Tawfiq (Success) and Al-Isma (Infallibility) are the creation of the power of obedience, and al-khidhlan (abandonment) is the creation of the power of disobedience. So the one granted success (al-muwaffaq) does not disobey, and vice versa. And it was said: Al-isma (1) is that God Almighty does not create sin in the servant; and it was said: it is a property by virtue of which the occurrence of sin from him is prevented. And the philosophers said: it is a disposition (malaka) that prevents immorality despite the ability to do it. And the Mu'tazila said: Al-lutuf is that by which the obligated person chooses obedience, whether by abandoning or performing, or which brings him close to them, while he has ability in both cases, and they are called al-muhassil (the effective) and al-muqarrib (the proximate); and the effective for the obligatory is specifically called al-tawfiq, and for abandoning the evil is called al-isma. And it was said: Al-tawfiq is the creation of a grace (lutf) by which God knows that the servant will obey; and al-khidhlan is the withholding of grace; and al-isma is a grace with which there is no motive to abandon obedience nor to commit disobedience, despite ability to both. They said: And grace varies according to the variation of obligated persons, and there is not in His knowledge that which is grace for everyone. And from this they interpreted the will (al-mashi'a) in the like of His saying 'And if We had willed, We would have given every soul its guidance' (2) as a will of compulsion and coercion. The creation of the power of obedience and al-khidhlan is the creation of the power of disobedience; and al-isma is exactly al-tawfiq. If it is general, it is general tawfiq; if it is specific, it is specific tawfiq. Thus mentioned it Imam al-Haramayn (3). And he said: Then the one granted success does not disobey since he has no power to disobey, and vice versa.
Source pages p. 312

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of key theological terms: al-lutf (grace), al-tawfiq (divine success), al-isma (infallibility), and al-khidhlan (abandonment). It presents the views of different groups: the Ash'aris (implicitly), the philosophers, and the Mu'tazila, and concludes with a statement from Imam al-Haramayn. The passage begins by stating that al-lutf, al-tawfiq, and al-isma are the creation of the power of obedience, while al-khidhlan is the creation of the power of disobedience. This sets the deterministic framework: success means God creates the ability to obey, and failure means He creates the ability to disobey. Then it reports various definitions of 'isma: some say it is that God does not create sin in the servant; others say it is a property preventing sin; the philosophers say it is a disposition that prevents vice despite ability. The Mu'tazila define lutf as that which leads the obligated one to choose obedience or get close to it, with ability preserved. They distinguish between effective (muhassil) and proximate (muqarrib) grace. The effective grace for obligatory acts is called tawfiq, and for abandoning evil is called 'isma. Another definition says tawfiq is the creation of grace that God knows will lead to obedience, khidhlan is withholding that grace, and 'isma is grace that leaves no motive to abandon obedience or commit sin. The passage notes that lutf varies by individual, and that God's knowledge does not contain a lutf that benefits everyone. This leads the Mu'tazila to interpret divine will in verses like 'If We willed, We would give every soul its guidance' as a will of compulsion. Finally, the passage returns to the Ash'ari view of Imam al-Haramayn: 'isma is identical to tawfiq; if general it is general success, if specific it is specific success. And the one granted success does not disobey because he lacks the power to disobey.

Key Terms
اللطف
التوفيق
العصمة
الخذلان
المحصل
المقرب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'isma means sinlessness as an inherent quality of a prophet, independent of God's creation.
Correction: The passage shows that 'isma is a grace (lutf) created by God that removes the motive to sin or prevents sin, not an intrinsic property.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila, what term is used for the effective grace that leads to abandoning evil?
A) Al-tawfiq
B) Al-isma
C) Al-khidhlan
D) Al-muqarrib

Key Takeaway
Grace (lutf) is the divine means by which obedience is chosen; success (tawfiq) and infallibility (isma) are its specific forms for obligatory acts and avoiding evil respectively.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you succeed in obeying a difficult command, do you genuinely feel that God's grace (lutf) enabled that choice, or do you credit your own willpower? What would change in your worship if you truly believed every act of obedience is a gift of tawfiq?
?
Think of a sin you have struggled to abandon. According to the definition of isma as a grace that removes motive to sin, why has that grace not been granted to you? Does your lack of isma relieve you of responsibility, or make the need for repentance more urgent?
?
The Mu'tazila argue that lutf varies by individual. If God knows exactly what lutf would lead you to obey, and He does not send it for some commands, are you content with that? What does that reveal about your relationship with His wisdom?

Imam al-Haramayn on the Identity of Isma and Tawfiq
Imam al-Haramayn's equation of isma and tawfiq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam al-Haramayn's view that 'isma is identical to tawfiq, and how this leads to the conclusion that the one granted success cannot disobey.

Core Idea
Infallibility is simply success by another name.

Imam al-Haramayn, a leading Ash'ari theologian, synthesizes the discussion by equating 'isma with tawfiq. This lesson examines his statement and its implications for understanding divine causation and human action.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the key claim
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the central claim: al-isma is exactly al-tawfiq. Then note the distinction between general and specific tawfiq.
2
Understand the consequence
Imam al-Haramayn concludes that the muwaffaq (one granted success) does not disobey because he lacks the power to disobey. This is a deterministic point.
This aligns with the Ash'ari view that all power is created by God.
3
Compare with prior definitions
Compare this view with the Mu'tazilite distinction between tawfiq and 'isma. How does Imam al-Haramayn collapse that distinction?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثاني اللطف والتوفيق والعصمة خلق قدرة الطاعة والخذلان خلق قدرة المعصية فالموفق لا يعصي وبالعكس وقيل العصمة (١) أن لا يخلق الله تعالى في العبد الذنب، وقيل خاصية يمتنع بسببه صدور الذنب عنه، وقالت الفلاسفة ملكة تمنع الفجور مع القدرة عليه، وقالت المعتزلة اللطف ما يختار المكلف عنده الطاعة تركاً أو إتياناً أو يقرب منهما مع تمكنه في الحالين، ويسميان المحصل والمقرب، ويختص المحصل للواجب باسم التوفيق، وترك القبيح باسم العصمة، وقيل: التوفيق : خلق لطف يعلم الله أن العبد يطيع عنده والخذلان منع اللطف والعصمة لطف لا داعي معه إلى ترك الطاعة ولا إلى ارتكاب المعصية مع القدرة عليهما . قالوا : واللطف يختلف باختلاف المكلفين، وليس في معلومه ما هو لطف في حق الكل ومن ههنا حملوا المشيئة في مثل قوله تعالى ولو شئنا لآتينا كل نفس هداها ) (۲) على مشيئة قسر وإلجاء) . خلق قدرة الطاعة والخذلان خلق قدرة المعصية، والعصمة هي التوفيق بعينه، فإن عممت كانت توفيقاً عاماً، وإن خصصت كانت توفيقاً خاصاً كذا ذكره إمام الحرمين (۳) وقال : ثم الموفق لا يعصي إذ لا قدرة له على المعصية وبالعكس
The Second Topic: Al-Lutuf (Grace) and Al-Tawfiq (Success) and Al-Isma (Infallibility) are the creation of the power of obedience, and al-khidhlan (abandonment) is the creation of the power of disobedience. So the one granted success (al-muwaffaq) does not disobey, and vice versa. And it was said: Al-isma (1) is that God Almighty does not create sin in the servant; and it was said: it is a property by virtue of which the occurrence of sin from him is prevented. And the philosophers said: it is a disposition (malaka) that prevents immorality despite the ability to do it. And the Mu'tazila said: Al-lutuf is that by which the obligated person chooses obedience, whether by abandoning or performing, or which brings him close to them, while he has ability in both cases, and they are called al-muhassil (the effective) and al-muqarrib (the proximate); and the effective for the obligatory is specifically called al-tawfiq, and for abandoning the evil is called al-isma. And it was said: Al-tawfiq is the creation of a grace (lutf) by which God knows that the servant will obey; and al-khidhlan is the withholding of grace; and al-isma is a grace with which there is no motive to abandon obedience nor to commit disobedience, despite ability to both. They said: And grace varies according to the variation of obligated persons, and there is not in His knowledge that which is grace for everyone. And from this they interpreted the will (al-mashi'a) in the like of His saying 'And if We had willed, We would have given every soul its guidance' (2) as a will of compulsion and coercion. The creation of the power of obedience and al-khidhlan is the creation of the power of disobedience; and al-isma is exactly al-tawfiq. If it is general, it is general tawfiq; if it is specific, it is specific tawfiq. Thus mentioned it Imam al-Haramayn (3). It presents the views of different groups: the Ash'aris (implicitly), the philosophers, and the Mu'tazila, and concludes with a statement from Imam al-Haramayn. Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'isma and tawfiq are distinct types of grace independent of each other.
Correction: Imam al-Haramayn teaches that they are identical; 'isma is just a general form of tawfiq.

Checkpoint
According to Imam al-Haramayn, what is the relationship between 'isma and tawfiq?
A) They are distinct types of grace
B) 'isma is a subset of tawfiq
C) 'isma is exactly tawfiq
D) Tawfiq is a subset of 'isma

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Haramayn equates infallibility with success, both being the same divine grace that prevents sin for lack of power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If infallibility is simply success from God, then what does it mean for your daily struggle with sin? Are you actively seeking God's success, or are you relying on your own willpower?
?
Consider a time you committed a sin. According to this teaching, does that mean you were abandoned by God? How does that make you feel, and how should it affect your supplication?
?
Do you truly believe that you cannot obey without God's success? If so, why do you often forget to ask Him for it before acts of worship?

The Mu'tazila Definition and Classification of Luṭf
Defines luṭf and classifies it into muqarrib and muḥaṣṣil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazila define luṭf and its two main categories: approximating (muqarrib) and effecting (muḥaṣṣil).

Core Idea
Luṭf is divine grace that guides choice while preserving human capability.

The Mu'tazila developed a sophisticated theory of divine grace (luṭf) to explain how God influences human actions without overriding free will. This lesson introduces their definition of luṭf and its fundamental division into two types. Understanding this classification is essential for grasping later debates on predestination and human responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the conditional 'in kāna' structures.
2
Identify the definition of luṭf
Find the sentence that defines luṭf. What are the key elements? Note that it mentions both 'chooses' and 'draws near'.
The phrase 'مع تمكنه في الحالين' is crucial.
3
Distinguish between muqarrib and muḥaṣṣil
Observe the two types and their conditions. What does each type achieve?
The difference is whether it actually secures the act or only brings near.
4
Reflect on the condition of capability
Ask yourself why the Mu'tazila insist on the person being 'mutamakkin' (capable) in both states. How does this preserve free will?
This is a key point of difference with other theological schools.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي كلام المعتزلة أن اللطف ما يختار المكلف عنده الطاعة تركاً أو إتياناً أو يقرب منهما مع تمكنه في الحالين، فإن كان مقرباً من الواجب أو ترك القبيح يسمى لطفاً مقرباً وإن كان محصلاً له فلطفاً محصلاً، ويخص المحصل للواجب باسم التوفيق، والمحصل لترك القبيح باسم العصمة ، ومنهم من قال : التوفيق خلق اللطف بعلم الله تعالى أن العبد يطيع عنده ، والخذلان منع اللطف، والعصمة لطف لا يكون معه داع إلى ترك الطاعة ولا إلى ارتكاب المعصية مع القدرة عليهما، واللطف هو الفعل الذي يعلم الله تعالى أن العبد يطيع عنده .
And in the speech of the Muʿtazila: al-luṭf (grace) is that by which the mukallaf (accountable person) chooses obedience, abandonment or commission, or draws near to them, while being capable in both states. If it brings near to the obligatory or to abandoning the reprehensible, it is called luṭf muqarrib (approximating grace); if it actually brings about [the act] it is luṭf muḥaṣṣil (effecting grace). And the effecting [grace] for the obligatory is named al-tawfīq (divine granting of success), and the effecting [grace] for abandoning the reprehensible is named al-ʿiṣma (infallibility). And among them is one who said: al-tawfīq is the creation of luṭf with Allah’s knowledge that the servant will obey through it; al-khidhlān (divine abandonment) is the withholding of luṭf; and al-ʿiṣma is a luṭf with which there is no motive to abandon obedience nor to commit disobedience, while having power over both. And al-luṭf is the act by which Allah knows that the servant will obey.
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage expounds the Muʿtazila theory of al-luṭf (divine grace). It defines luṭf as an act of God that leads the morally responsible person to choose obedience—either by doing it or abandoning it—or draws them close to that choice, while the person remains capable of both obedience and disobedience. It then classifies luṭf into two types: muqarrib (approximating) which brings the person near to performing an obligatory act or to abandoning a reprehensible one, and muḥaṣṣil (effecting) which actually secures the act or abandonment. The muḥaṣṣil type is further specified: when it secures the performance of an obligation it is called tawfīq (divine success), and when it secures the abandonment of a sin it is called ʿiṣma (infallibility). The passage then reports an alternative view among Muʿtazila: tawfīq is the creation of luṭf with God's knowledge that the servant will obey through it; khidhlān (divine abandonment) is the withholding of luṭf; ʿiṣma is a luṭf that leaves no motive either to abandon obedience or to commit sin, while the person still has power over both. The passage concludes by affirming that luṭf is an act of God by which He knows that the servant will obey.

Key Terms
لطف
مقرب
محصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Luṭf overrides human free will.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that the person remains capable in both states, so luṭf does not compel.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila, when is luṭf called muqarrib?
A) When it actually brings about the act of obedience.
B) When it brings the person near to the obligatory or to abandoning the reprehensible.
C) When it secures the performance of an obligation.
D) When it is withheld by God.

Key Takeaway
Luṭf is divine grace that assists the morally responsible person toward obedience while preserving their capability, and it is classified as either approximating (muqarrib) or effecting (muḥaṣṣil).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When was the last time you felt a 'luṭf muqarrib'—a situation that made obedience easier but still required your choice? Did you take the step, or did you resist? What does your response reveal about your reliance on God's grace versus your own efforts?
?
If you truly believed that every moment of obedience is enabled by a specific grace from God (luṭf), how would your attitude toward missed acts of worship change? Would you be more grateful for your successes and more humble about your failures?
?
The Mu'tazila insist that luṭf never robs you of capability—you are always equally able to disobey. In what area of your life have you used 'but God made it easy for me' as an excuse to not take full moral responsibility for your choices?

Subtypes of Luṭf: Tawfīq, ʿIṣma, and Khidhlān
Specifies subtypes of muḥaṣṣil and presents alternative definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the specific terms tawfīq, ʿiṣma, and khidhlān within the Mu'tazila theory of luṭf.

Core Idea
Tawfīq and ʿiṣma are subtypes of effecting grace; khidhlān is the withholding of grace.

After establishing the general classification of luṭf, the Mu'tazila further specify the effecting type with two important terms: tawfīq (divine success) for obligations and ʿiṣma (infallibility) for avoiding sin. They also discuss the opposite concept, khidhlān (divine abandonment), and an alternative definition of luṭf. This lesson explores these subtle distinctions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note how it continues from the previous classification.
This passage builds directly on the definition of muḥaṣṣil.
2
Identify the specific names
Find where tawfīq and ʿiṣma are assigned to the two subtypes of effecting grace.
One is for obligations, the other for avoiding sin.
3
Understand the alternative view
Read the alternative definition of tawfīq and the introduction of khidhlān. How do they relate?
Khidhlān is simply the absence of luṭf.
4
Reflect on the definition of ʿiṣma
Note that ʿiṣma is described as leaving no motive (dāʿ) to disobey, yet the person still has power. Why is this significant?
This preserves moral responsibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي كلام المعتزلة أن اللطف ما يختار المكلف عنده الطاعة تركاً أو إتياناً أو يقرب منهما مع تمكنه في الحالين، فإن كان مقرباً من الواجب أو ترك القبيح يسمى لطفاً مقرباً وإن كان محصلاً له فلطفاً محصلاً، ويخص المحصل للواجب باسم التوفيق، والمحصل لترك القبيح باسم العصمة ، ومنهم من قال : التوفيق خلق اللطف بعلم الله تعالى أن العبد يطيع عنده ، والخذلان منع اللطف، والعصمة لطف لا يكون معه داع إلى ترك الطاعة ولا إلى ارتكاب المعصية مع القدرة عليهما، واللطف هو الفعل الذي يعلم الله تعالى أن العبد يطيع عنده .
And in the speech of the Muʿtazila: al-luṭf (grace) is that by which the mukallaf (accountable person) chooses obedience, abandonment or commission, or draws near to them, while being capable in both states. If it brings near to the obligatory or to abandoning the reprehensible, it is called luṭf muqarrib (approximating grace); if it actually brings about [the act] it is luṭf muḥaṣṣil (effecting grace). And the effecting [grace] for the obligatory is named al-tawfīq (divine granting of success), and the effecting [grace] for abandoning the reprehensible is named al-ʿiṣma (infallibility). And among them is one who said: al-tawfīq is the creation of luṭf with Allah’s knowledge that the servant will obey through it; al-khidhlān (divine abandonment) is the withholding of luṭf; and al-ʿiṣma is a luṭf with which there is no motive to abandon obedience nor to commit disobedience, while having power over both. And al-luṭf is the act by which Allah knows that the servant will obey.
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage expounds the Muʿtazila theory of al-luṭf (divine grace). It defines luṭf as an act of God that leads the morally responsible person to choose obedience—either by doing it or abandoning it—or draws them close to that choice, while the person remains capable of both obedience and disobedience. It then classifies luṭf into two types: muqarrib (approximating) which brings the person near to performing an obligatory act or to abandoning a reprehensible one, and muḥaṣṣil (effecting) which actually secures the act or abandonment. The muḥaṣṣil type is further specified: when it secures the performance of an obligation it is called tawfīq (divine success), and when it secures the abandonment of a sin it is called ʿiṣma (infallibility). The passage then reports an alternative view among Muʿtazila: tawfīq is the creation of luṭf with God's knowledge that the servant will obey through it; khidhlān (divine abandonment) is the withholding of luṭf; ʿiṣma is a luṭf that leaves no motive either to abandon obedience or to commit sin, while the person still has power over both. The passage concludes by affirming that luṭf is an act of God by which He knows that the servant will obey.

Key Terms
توفيق
عصمة
خذلان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: ʿIṣma removes the ability to sin, making the person incapable of disobedience.
Correction: The text explicitly states 'مع القدرة عليهما' (with power over both), so the person remains capable.

Checkpoint
According to the alternative view mentioned, what is khidhlān?
A) A luṭf that effects obedience.
B) The withholding of luṭf.
C) A luṭf that brings near to obedience.
D) The creation of luṭf.

Key Takeaway
Within Mu'tazila theology, tawfīq and ʿiṣma are specific forms of effecting grace, while khidhlān is the withholding of grace; all preserve human capability.

First Mu'tazilite Proof for Lutf and Its Refutation
Sanusi presents the first Mu'tazilite proof for lutf and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first Mu'tazilite proof for the obligation of lutf and how Ash'ari scholars refuted it by denying the entailment and the equation of command with will.

Core Idea
God's command does not always reflect His will.

The Mu'tazilites argued that because God desires obedience, He must provide the means (lutf). This lesson presents that argument and the Ash'ari response, which challenges both the logical link and the assumption that everything commanded is willed. Understanding this refutation clarifies the Ash'ari distinction between divine command and will.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the concise argument structure.
The proof uses a conditional: if X then contradiction.
2
Analyze the Mu'tazilite proof
Identify the premises: God wills obedience; if He withholds lutf, He would not will obedience → contradiction. So lutf must be provided.
The proof assumes that preventing the means implies not willing the end.
3
Examine the refutation
The refutation denies two things: the entailment (mulāzama) and the premise that every commanded act is willed. Understand why these denials break the argument.
Ash'aris hold that God can command without willing (e.g., command to disbelievers).
4
Consider the broader significance
This refutation shows that the obligation of lutf does not follow from God's command alone. The concept of lutf remains a grace, not an obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : أنه مريد للطاعة، فلو جاز منع ما يحصل أو يقرب منها لكان غير مريد لها، وهو تناقض ، ورد بمنع الملازمة ومنع أن(١) كل مأمور به مراد.
The first: that He wants obedience, so if it were permissible to prevent that which obtains or brings near to it, then He would not want it, which is a contradiction. It was refuted by denying the entailment and denying that every commanded thing is wanted.
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage presents the first Mu'tazilite proof for the obligation of lutf and its refutation. The proof argues that God desires obedience from humans. If it were permissible for Him to withhold something that obtains or brings near obedience (i.e., lutf), then He would not truly desire that obedience, which is contradictory. Therefore, lutf must be obligatory. The refutation (al-radd) denies two things: first, the logical entailment (al-mulāzama) between desiring obedience and being obliged to provide means; second, the premise that every commanded thing is actually willed by God. God may command something without willing it from a particular person, or He may will it conditionally. Thus the proof fails.

Key Terms
الملازمة
المأمور به
مراد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's command necessarily implies that He wills the commanded act from the specific person.
Correction: Ash'aris hold that God may command something (e.g., faith) without willing it from a particular individual; command pertains to what is requested, will pertains to what occurs.

Checkpoint
How does the refutation respond to the first proof for the obligation of lutf?
A) It accepts the proof but argues lutf is not needed.
B) It denies that God wants obedience.
C) It denies the entailment (mulāzama) and that every commanded thing is willed.
D) It denies that lutf brings near to obedience.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite proof fails because Ash'aris deny that divine command necessarily implies divine will, and they deny the logical entailment from willing an end to providing all means.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Mu'tazilite assumed that God's desire for obedience naturally obligates Him to provide all necessary helps. Do you live as if your own desires for good things obligate God to make them easy for you? What does this reveal about your trust in His wisdom?
?
Ash'aris teach that God may command something without willing it from a particular person. When you read this, does it unsettle you? Could it be that some of your acts of obedience are not truly willed by Allah for you? How does that affect your humility before Him?
?
If God's command does not always reflect His will, then your obedience is not simply about fulfilling a checklist. It is about aligning your heart with the deeper will of Allah. What is one command you have been obeying mechanically? Will you today seek to obey it with the awareness that His will for you is more than the outer act?

Second Proof for Lutf and Its Refutation
Sanusi presents the second Mu'tazilite proof for lutf and refutes it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second Mu'tazilite proof for the obligation of lutf and how al-Sanusi refutes it by denying its premises.

Core Idea
Preventing lutf does not necessarily frustrate God's purpose; frustration may serve other wise purposes.

Al-Sanusi now turns to the second argument that the Mu'tazilites use to claim that God must grant lutf (grace) to enable obedience. This argument rests on the notion that withholding lutf would undermine God's purpose. In this lesson, you will see the argument and the incisive refutation that exposes its hidden assumptions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get the overall flow of the argument and refutation.
Notice the concise style: the proof is stated in one sentence, the refutation in another.
2
Identify the structure: proof and refutation
The passage has two parts: the Mu'tazilite proof (first sentence) and al-Sanusi's refutation (second sentence). Mark where each begins.
3
Analyze the proof's two premises
The proof relies on two premises: (1) preventing lutf frustrates God's purpose (getting the commanded act done), (2) frustrating a purpose is evil and must be avoided. Confirm these in the text.
The word 'نقض الغرض' (frustrating the purpose) is key.
4
Understand the refutation
The refutation denies both premises: it is possible that frustrating the commanded act is not God's purpose at all, and that frustration may carry divine rulings and benefits. Reflect on how this undercuts the proof.
This shows that the Mu'tazilite assumption is not necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : أن منع اللطف نقض لغرضه الذي هو الإتيان بالمأمور به، ونقض الغرض (۲) قبيح يجب تركه ورد بمنع المقدمتين لجواز أن لا يكون نقض المأمور به مراداً أو غرضاً، ويتعلق بنقضه (۳) حكم ومصالح .
The second: that preventing lutf frustrates His purpose which is the performance of the commanded act, and frustrating the purpose is ugly and must be avoided. It was refuted by denying both premises, because it is possible that frustrating the commanded act is not intended or a purpose, and there may attach to its frustration a ruling and benefits.
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage presents the second Mu'tazilite proof: preventing lutf frustrates God's purpose, which is the performance of the commanded act. Since frustrating a purpose is evil, it must be avoided, so lutf is obligatory. The refutation denies both premises: first, it is possible that God's purpose is not to bring about the commanded act in every case; second, frustrating that act may serve other wise purposes and benefits. Thus the proof fails.

Key Terms
اللطف
الغرض
نقض الغرض
قبيح
المقدمتين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's purpose must always be fulfilled exactly as intended, so any frustration is impossible.
Correction: Ash'aris hold that God's will is not constrained by human notions of purpose; what appears as frustration may serve higher wisdom and divine rulings.

Checkpoint
What is the refutation's main point against the second proof?
A) God has no purpose
B) Preventing lutf is not frustrating any purpose
C) Frustrating the commanded act may not be God's purpose and may have benefits
D) The commanded act is always performed

Key Takeaway
The second proof for lutf fails because it assumes God's purpose is necessarily tied to each commanded act, whereas Ash'aris maintain that God's will is not subject to such necessity and may have other wise purposes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that God's purpose may not be to see you perform every commanded act in this very moment. Is there a command of God you are currently struggling to fulfill? Will you still trust His wisdom even if you never see the outcome you expect?
?
The Mu'tazilites thought they could box God into a human definition of purpose. Where in your life have you assumed God 'must' act a certain way? Are you willing to surrender that presumption and submit to His unconstrained will?
?
This lesson teaches that even the frustration of a commanded act can have divine rulings and benefits. What trial or failure in your obedience might actually be a hidden mercy from God? Commit today to asking Him for insight rather than demanding success.

Counterargument Against Lutf: No Disbelievers if Lutf Were Obligatory
Sanusi presents a Mu'tazilite counterargument and refutes it with Qur'anic texts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite counterargument that obligatory lutf would imply universal faith, and how Sanusi refutes it by citing Qur'anic texts that ground faith in God's will.

Core Idea
Divine will, not lutf, determines faith.

This passage presents a critical objection against the Mu'tazilite doctrine of lutf. If lutf were obligatory, God would provide it for all, eliminating disbelief. The Mu'tazilites try to answer by noting that lutf varies among individuals, but Sanusi refutes them with Qur'anic verses showing that universal faith depends on God's will. This lesson unpacks the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the counterargument
Read the first part of the passage: 'if lutf were obligatory, there would remain no disbeliever nor sinner.' Why does this follow from the Mu'tazilite rule that the utmost lutf is obligatory?
The key is the concept of 'muhassil' lutf that effects belief.
2
Consider the Mu'tazilite answer
The Mu'tazilites respond that even disbelievers have lutf, but the counterargument still stands. Then they offer a deeper answer: lutf differs per person. Why does this seem to save their position?
They argue that no single lutf works for all.
3
Examine the refutation
Sanusi refutes this answer by quoting Qur'anic verses like 'If We had willed, We would have given every soul its guidance.' How do these verses show that the issue is God's will, not lutf?
God clearly has the power to give effective guidance to all.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this refutation undermines the Mu'tazilite position. If God can will universal faith but does not, then lutf is not the determinant. What does this imply about divine justice and will?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : أنه لو وجب اللطف لما بقي كافر ولا فاسق لأن من الألطاف ما هو محصل ومن قواعدهم أن أقصى اللطف واجب، فلا يندفع ما ذكرنا بما قيل إن الكافر أو الفاسق لا يخلو عن لطف ، فلذا () أجيب : بأن اللطف يتفاوت بالنسبة إلى المكلفين، وليس كل ما هو لطف في إيمان زيد لطف في إيمان عمرو، فليس في معلوم الله تعالى ما هو لطف في حق الكل، حتى يحصل إيمانهم . وردّ بالنصوص الدالة على أن انتفاء إيمان الكل مبني على انتفاء مشيئة الله ، وذلك كقوله تعالى ولو شئنا لآتينا كل نفس هداها (٥) و ولو شاء ربك لجعل الناس أمة واحدة (٦) فلو شاء لهداكم أجمعين (۷) إلى غير ذلك مما لا يحصى سيما في
The first: that if lutf were obligatory, there would remain no disbeliever nor sinner, because among the acts of lutf are those that are effecting (muhassil), and it is one of their rules that the utmost lutf is obligatory. So what we mentioned is not repelled by what was said: 'the disbeliever or sinner is not devoid of lutf.' For that reason it was answered: that lutf varies with respect to the morally responsible, and not every lutf that is for the faith of Zayd is a lutf for the faith of 'Amr. So in God's knowledge there is no lutf that is suitable for all such that their faith would occur. And it was refuted by texts indicating that the absence of faith in all is based on the absence of God's will, such as His saying: 'And if We had willed, We would have given every soul its guidance' (32:12), and 'If your Lord had willed, He would have made mankind one community' (11:118), and 'If He had willed, He would have guided you all' (6:149), and many other verses especially in
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage presents a counterargument (mu'āraḍa) against the Mu'tazilite proofs: if lutf were obligatory, God would have provided it for everyone, resulting in universal belief and obedience, eliminating all disbelief and sin. Some Mu'tazila respond that even disbelievers receive some lutf, but the author objects that this does not undermine the conclusion. The Mu'tazilite answer to this counterargument is that lutf varies among individuals; what is lutf for one person may not be lutf for another. Therefore, there is no single lutf that would guarantee faith for all. However, the author refutes this answer by quoting Qur'anic verses that attribute the absence of universal faith to God's will, not to a lack of suitable lutf. Verses like 'If We had willed, We would have given every soul its guidance' show that God could have provided effective guidance if He willed. Thus, the Mu'tazilite position is contradicted by revelation.

Key Terms
المعارضة
أقصى اللطف
المشيئة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if lutf varies per person, God cannot provide universal guidance because a single lutf does not suit all.
Correction: The refutation shows that God could have given each person an individually suited lutf if He willed; the Qur'an attributes universal faith to God's will, not to lutf.

Checkpoint

A) By denying that lutf varies
B) By citing Qur'anic verses showing that universal guidance depends on God's will
C) By arguing that lutf is not needed
D) By saying that even varied lutf would still be insufficient

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite claim that lutf is obligatory is refuted by the Qur'anic teaching that universal faith depends on God's will, not on the availability of lutf.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's will is the ultimate cause of faith, what does that mean for the times you felt your own efforts were the reason for your iman? Will you now shift your reliance from your own striving to God's mercy alone, and how will that change your daily du'ā?
?
The Mu'tazilites thought God was obliged to give everyone what they need to believe. But the Qur'an says God does not will universal faith. Are you harboring any hidden resentment against God for not guiding someone you love, or for not making your own faith easier? Can you submit to His will even when it doesn't fit your sense of fairness?
?
This passage shows that even the best 'lutf' cannot guarantee faith without God's willing it. In your life, what 'lutf' (blessings, sermons, good company) have you received that still have not produced the level of taqwā you desire? Will you today stop blaming your circumstances and instead plead for God's mashī'a to change your heart?

When Is Compensation Given? Timing and Scope
Sanusi presents the disagreement on the timing and scope of compensation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the disagreement over whether compensation (awad) must be in the hereafter or can be in this world, and whether it encompasses sins by analogy with reward.

Core Idea
Compensation's timing and scope are debated: hereafter or this world? Encompassing sins or not?

Sanusi begins by outlining a key disagreement among theologians: is compensation necessarily reserved for the afterlife, or can it be granted in this world? And if it is given, does it cover all sins, similar to how reward covers good deeds? This passage sets the stage for deeper discussions about divine justice and wisdom.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the two main questions: timing (hereafter vs. this world) and scope (encompassing sins or not).
The phrase 'اعتباراً بالثواب' means 'by analogy with reward'.
2
Identify the two opinions
Identify the two contrasting views: (1) compensation must be in the hereafter and encompasses sins; (2) compensation may be in this world and does not encompass sins at all.
The second view is supported by 'lac of evidence for the contrary'.
3
Reflect on the implications
Think about why this matters: if compensation is only in the hereafter, then suffering in this world has no immediate compensation, but if it can be in this world, then divine justice might be more immediate. What does this say about God's wisdom?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها اختلافهم في أن العوض هل يجب أن يكون في الآخرة، وهل يحيط بالذنوب اعتباراً بالثواب، أم يجوز في الدنيا ، ولا يحيط أصلاً لعدم الدليل على النقيض، وفي أنه هل يجوز التفضل بمثل الأعواض ابتداء من غير سبق ألم أم لا؟ وعلى تقدير الجواز هل يجوز الإيلام وتحسن المحن لمجرد العوض؟ كما هو رأي أبي علي بناء على أن للعوض اللازم للمستحق مزية على المتفضل به من غير لزوم، واستحقاق أم لا بد مع ذلك من يكون ألطافاً للمؤلم في الزجر عن القبيح؟ ولغيره بحسب الاتعاظ والاعتبار؟ كما هو رأي الضميري، أم لا بد من كلا الأمرين كما هو رأي أبي هاشم بناء على أنه لما جاز مثل العوض ابتداء كان الإسلام لمجرد العوض عبثاً خارجاً عن الحكمة، وما يقال من أن للمستحق اللازم مزية على المتفضل به الغير اللازم فإنما هو في حق من يوقف من تفضله .
Among them is their disagreement: Is compensation necessarily in the hereafter, and does it encompass sins by analogy with reward, or is it permissible in this world, and does not encompass at all due to lack of evidence for the contrary? And whether it is permissible to bestow the like of compensations initially without prior pain or not? And assuming permissibility, is inflicting pain and trials good solely for compensation? As is the view of Abi Ali, based on the fact that the obligatory compensation due to the deserving has an advantage over what is freely bestowed without obligation; or is it necessary along with that to have benefits (alṭāf) for the pained person in deterring from evil, and for others according to admonition and consideration? As is the view of al-Damiri; or must both matters be present as is the view of Abi Hashim, based on the fact that when compensation is permissible initially, accepting Islam merely for compensation would be futile, outside wisdom. And what is said that the obligatory compensation due to the deserving has an advantage over the freely bestowed which is not obligatory, that is only in the case of one who refrains from His favor.
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage presents several disagreements among theologians regarding compensation (العوض). It first questions whether compensation must be in the afterlife or can be given in this world, whether it encompasses all sins by analogy with reward, or whether it is not given at all due to lack of evidence. Then it discusses whether God can bestow the like of compensations initially without prior pain. Assuming permissibility, does inflicting pain and trials become good solely for compensation? That is the view of Abi Ali, based on the idea that obligatory compensation due to the deserving has an advantage over voluntary bestowal without obligation. Or is it necessary that along with compensation there be benefits (ألطاف) for the pained person in deterring from evil, and for others as lessons and admonitions? That is the view of al-Damiri. Or must both compensation and benefits be present? That is the view of Abi Hashim, based on the reasoning that since it is permissible to give compensation initially, accepting Islam merely for compensation would be futile and contrary to wisdom. The argument that the obligatory compensation due to the deserving has an advantage over voluntary bestowal is only valid for one who refrains from His favor.

Key Terms
العوض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that compensation must always be in the afterlife, but the text shows a valid opinion that it can be in this world.
Correction: The passage explicitly presents the view that compensation may be given in this world, based on lack of evidence for the contrary. So it is not a settled matter.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for the view that compensation does not encompass sins?
A) It is forbidden by revelation.
B) There is no evidence for the contrary.
C) It would lead to injustice.
D) Sins are too great to be compensated.

Key Takeaway
The timing and scope of compensation are debated: some say it is necessarily in the hereafter and covers all sins, while others say it may be in this world and does not cover sins at all.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face hardship in this world, do you ever see it as a form of compensation or punishment for your sins? How does the possibility that compensation may not cover all sins change your understanding of your trials? Will you continue to read them as divine messages, or will you let this uncertainty drive you to deeper repentance?
?
If compensation could be delayed entirely to the hereafter, does that make you more complacent about your sins, knowing they might be 'paid for' later? Or does the mere possibility that compensation might not cover them at all shake you from that comfort? What will you change today because of this divine ambiguity?

Is Compensation Enough? The Need for Benefits
Sanusi presents three positions on whether compensation must be accompanied by benefits (alṭāf).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three positions on whether compensation alone justifies suffering, or whether it must be accompanied by additional benefits (alṭāf).

Core Idea
Compensation alone may not justify suffering; benefits may be needed to deter evil or provide lessons.

We now move to a more refined disagreement: assuming compensation is permissible, does inflicting pain become good solely because of the compensation? Or must there be additional benefits—like deterring the sufferer from evil or serving as a lesson for others? Three theologians offer distinct answers, revealing deep differences in how divine justice and wisdom are understood.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage for this lesson
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. This lesson covers the second half of the original passage, starting from 'وفي أنه هل يجوز التفضل...'.
2
Identify the three views
Look for the three scholars: Abi Ali, al-Damiri, and Abu Hashim. For each, note what condition they require for compensation to be justifiable.
Abi Ali: compensation alone is enough. Al-Damiri: compensation plus benefits. Abu Hashim: both compensation and benefits are necessary.
3
Analyze the reasoning
Examine the reasoning behind each view. For example, Abu Hashim argues that if compensation could be given initially, then accepting Islam merely for compensation would be futile ('عبثاً'). This shows a concern for wisdom (حكمة).
4
Reflect on the broader implications
Consider how these views relate to the problem of evil and divine justice. Does compensation automatically justify any suffering? Or must suffering serve a higher purpose?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها اختلافهم في أن العوض هل يجب أن يكون في الآخرة، وهل يحيط بالذنوب اعتباراً بالثواب، أم يجوز في الدنيا ، ولا يحيط أصلاً لعدم الدليل على النقيض، وفي أنه هل يجوز التفضل بمثل الأعواض ابتداء من غير سبق ألم أم لا؟ وعلى تقدير الجواز هل يجوز الإيلام وتحسن المحن لمجرد العوض؟ كما هو رأي أبي علي بناء على أن للعوض اللازم للمستحق مزية على المتفضل به من غير لزوم، واستحقاق أم لا بد مع ذلك من يكون ألطافاً للمؤلم في الزجر عن القبيح؟ ولغيره بحسب الاتعاظ والاعتبار؟ كما هو رأي الضميري، أم لا بد من كلا الأمرين كما هو رأي أبي هاشم بناء على أنه لما جاز مثل العوض ابتداء كان الإسلام لمجرد العوض عبثاً خارجاً عن الحكمة، وما يقال من أن للمستحق اللازم مزية على المتفضل به الغير اللازم فإنما هو في حق من يوقف من تفضله .
Among them is their disagreement: Is compensation necessarily in the hereafter, and does it encompass sins by analogy with reward, or is it permissible in this world, and does not encompass at all due to lack of evidence for the contrary? And whether it is permissible to bestow the like of compensations initially without prior pain or not? And assuming permissibility, is inflicting pain and trials good solely for compensation? As is the view of Abi Ali, based on the fact that the obligatory compensation due to the deserving has an advantage over what is freely bestowed without obligation; or is it necessary along with that to have benefits (alṭāf) for the pained person in deterring from evil, and for others according to admonition and consideration? As is the view of al-Damiri; or must both matters be present as is the view of Abi Hashim, based on the fact that when compensation is permissible initially, accepting Islam merely for compensation would be futile, outside wisdom. And what is said that the obligatory compensation due to the deserving has an advantage over the freely bestowed which is not obligatory, that is only in the case of one who refrains from His favor.
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage presents several disagreements among theologians regarding compensation (العوض). It first questions whether compensation must be in the afterlife or can be given in this world, whether it encompasses all sins by analogy with reward, or whether it is not given at all due to lack of evidence. Then it discusses whether God can bestow the like of compensations initially without prior pain. Assuming permissibility, does inflicting pain and trials become good solely for compensation? That is the view of Abi Ali, based on the idea that obligatory compensation due to the deserving has an advantage over voluntary bestowal without obligation. Or is it necessary that along with compensation there be benefits (ألطاف) for the pained person in deterring from evil, and for others as lessons and admonitions? That is the view of al-Damiri. Or must both compensation and benefits be present? That is the view of Abi Hashim, based on the reasoning that since it is permissible to give compensation initially, accepting Islam merely for compensation would be futile and contrary to wisdom. The argument that the obligatory compensation due to the deserving has an advantage over voluntary bestowal is only valid for one who refrains from His favor.

Key Terms
العوض
الألطاف
أبو علي
الضميري
أبو هاشم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that any suffering is automatically justified if compensation is given, but the text shows that many scholars require additional purposes (benefits).
Correction: The views of al-Damiri and Abu Hashim indicate that compensation alone may not be sufficient; benefits like deterrence or lessons are necessary for the action to be wise and just.

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hashim, why is compensation alone insufficient?
A) Because compensation is never given in this world.
B) Because accepting Islam for mere compensation would be futile and outside wisdom.
C) Because benefits are always better than compensation.
D) Because only the deserving can receive compensation.

Key Takeaway
Theologians differ on whether compensation alone suffices to justify suffering: Abi Ali says yes, al-Damiri adds benefits, and Abu Hashim requires both compensation and benefits.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a trial or suffering, do you tend to focus on what compensation you might receive, or do you look for the benefits (alṭāf) in it? What does your tendency reveal about your relationship with God?
?
If God offered you a reward for every hardship but removed the opportunity for growth and deterrence from sin, would you be satisfied? How does the view of al-Damiri challenge your idea of what true compensation should include?
?
Abu Hashim says that acting for mere compensation is 'futile and outside wisdom.' Are there areas in your life where you serve God only for reward? What would it take to shift your intention to include the higher purposes of obedience?

Pains of Non-Rational Beings and Their Compensation
Mu'tazili view on pains of non-rational beings and their compensations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazili view on the goodness of pains for non-rational beings and the subsequent disagreements regarding their compensations and afterlife.

Core Idea
Pains of children, insane, and animals are good because they receive greater compensations.

The Mu'tazila held that God's actions are always good and wise, including the pains inflicted on non-rational beings. They argued such pains are compensated with rewards exceeding the pain, making them good. However, they disagreed on the timing and nature of these compensations. This passage presents their core agreement and the points of contention.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the flow from agreement to disagreements.
The author uses 'and then they disagreed' as a transition.
2
Identify the core agreement
The Mu'tazila agreed that pains of non-rational beings are good because of compensations that exceed the pain.
This is the baseline view.
3
List the points of disagreement
Find each point where they disagreed: timing, paradise, intellect, compensation nature, final end.
There are at least five distinct issues.
4
Reflect on the tafsir reference
The passage ends with a tafsir about the disbeliever's wish to be dust, linking to animal compensations.
This is a specific interpretation from some exegetes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أنهم ذهبوا إلى أن آلام غير العاقل من الصبيان والمجانين والبهائم حسنة لالتزام أعواض يزيد عليها، ثم اضطربوا في أنها تكون في الدنيا، أم في الآخرة. وفي أن البهائم هل تدخل الجنة؟ ويخلق فيها العقل والعلم، وأن ذلك عوض أم لا ؟ وفي أن عاقبة أمرها ماذا ؟ وفي بعض التفاسير أن قول الكافر يا ليتني كنت تراباً يكون حين يوصل الله تعالى إلى البهائم أعواضها، ثم يجعلها تراباً.
Among them is that they held that the pains of non-rational beings – children, the insane, and animals – are good because of the commitment of compensations exceeding them. Then they disagreed: Are they in this world or the hereafter? And do animals enter paradise? And are intellect and knowledge created in them? And is that a compensation or not? And what is their final end? And in some interpretations, the saying of the disbeliever 'Would that I were dust' occurs when God gives the animals their compensations, then makes them dust.
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This passage discusses the pains of non-rational beings: children, the insane, and animals. The Mu'tazila agree that such pains are good because they entail compensations that exceed the pain. However, they disagree on several points: whether compensations are given in this world or the afterlife, whether animals enter paradise, whether they are granted intellect and knowledge at that time, whether that is considered compensation, and what their ultimate fate is. The author mentions a tafsir that the disbeliever's wish to be dust occurs after God gives animals their compensations and then turns them to dust.

Key Terms

العوض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Correction: The text shows that Mu'tazila debated animals' entry into paradise and their ultimate fate, affirming some form of compensation.

Checkpoint

A) Whether they are good at all.
B) Whether compensations are in this world or the hereafter.
C) Whether God inflicts them.
D) Whether they are eternal.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila agreed that pains of non-rational beings are good due to compensations, but they disagreed on the specifics of those compensations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Mu'tazila reasoned that pains of the innocent are good because of compensations that exceed them. When you see a child suffering from a terminal illness, does this argument feel sufficient? Or does it expose a limitation in your own ability to trust God's justice without needing to 'calculate' the reward? How would you hold onto faith even when the equation seems unbalanced?
?
The disbeliever's wish to be dust (Q78:40) is interpreted here as happening after animals receive their compensations. If you were to stand before God and see perfect justice meted out, would there be any regret you carry now that you would wish to undo? What would you change today so that you face that moment with confidence rather than longing to be nothing?
?
If God's justice requires that every pain of an innocent creature is compensated beyond measure, how does that reshape your view of your own trials? Are you willing to accept that your own hardest moments will be more than repaid? What would it mean for you to live today as if that promise were already true?

Objection and Answer on Causing Pain Without Consent
Objection and answer on permissibility of causing pain without consent for greater good.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how classical kalam addresses the objection that if God can cause pain for a greater good, then humans could also claim that right, and the theological distinctions used to respond.

Core Idea
God's ability to fully compensate justifies pain that humans cannot.

This passage presents a classic objection against divine permission to cause pain without consent. The objector argues that if God can do it, so can humans. The response either accepts the consequence or distinguishes God's omniscience and ability to compensate. Additionally, the text notes that some theologians, like al-Damiri and the Mu'tazila, consider certain human-caused pains as rationally good when compensated.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and two answers
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp the objection and the two responses. Notice the structure: objection, then two possible answers.
The first answer is acceptance; the second is a distinction.
2
Understand the distinction
The key distinction is that God knows He can provide compensation, whereas a human servant cannot guarantee it. Reflect on why this matters for permissibility.
This distinction preserves God's unique justice.
3
Consider the Mu'tazila view
The passage references al-Damiri and the Mu'tazila who hold that causing pain for another's benefit is not rationally good, implying it requires revelation or compensation to be justified.
This shows diversity of opinion within Islamic theology.
4
Reflect on rational basis
Think about the rational principle: causing pain without consent can be good if there is a greater benefit and full compensation. Ask yourself how this applies to God vs. humans.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : فيلزم جواز ذلك للعبد أيضاً. أجيب بالتزامه أو بالفرق، فإن الله عالم بالتمكن من التعويض بخلاف العبد ، وأما الإيلام بدون الرضا لمنفعة الغير على ما يراه الضميري في إيلام زيد لاعتبار عمرو، وجمهور المعتزلة في ذبح الحيوانات واستعمالها لمنافع العباد، فلا يعقل ،حسنه
If it is said: Then that would also be permissible for the servant. It is answered by accepting it or by making a distinction: God knows that He is able to provide compensation, unlike the servant. As for inflicting pain without consent for the benefit of another, according to what al-Damiri holds in causing pain to Zayd for the sake of 'Amr, and the majority of the Mu'tazila regarding slaughtering animals and using them for the benefits of servants, its goodness is not rationally conceivable.
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This passage records an objection and two possible answers regarding the permissibility of causing pain without consent. The objector argues that if God can cause pain for a greater good, then a human might also claim that right. The answer either accepts the consequence or distinguishes God from humans because God can provide complete compensation. The text then mentions that al-Damiri and the Mu'tazila consider causing pain for the benefit of others to be not rationally good.

Key Terms
الإيلام
التعويض
الضميري
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the text affirms that causing pain without consent is always wrong, even for God.
Correction: The text shows that for God it is permissible because He can fully compensate, while for humans it is not generally permissible unless they can also guarantee compensation.

Checkpoint
What is the distinction given in the answer to the objection?
A) The servant can also provide compensation.
B) God is able to provide compensation, unlike the servant.
C) The servant is never allowed to cause pain.
D) The Mu'tazila reject the argument entirely.

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of causing pain without consent hinges on the possibility of full compensation, which only God can guarantee.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
If God alone can guarantee full compensation, what does that imply about your attempts to justify your actions by their consequences? Are you trusting your own limited knowledge or God's perfect justice?

The Principle of al-Ikhtiram (Cutting Short Life)
Discussion on al-ikhtiram with majority and Mu'tazila views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the principle of al-ikhtiram and why the majority of theologians hold that God is not obligated to cut short the life of one He knows will sin.

Core Idea
God's knowledge of future sin does not obligate Him to end life; sin is the servant's own act.

This lesson examines the fourth principle of al-ikhtiram, a debated topic in kalam. The question is whether God must end the life of a person He knows will commit sin if left alive. The passage presents the majority Ash'ari position against some Mu'tazila, grounding the argument in the nature of human responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Understand the question of al-ikhtiram
The text asks: if God knows that an infallible or repentant person will sin if kept alive, must He end that life? This is the core of the discussion.
Pay attention to the definitions: 'infallible' (معصوم) and 'repentant' (تائب).
2
Learn the majority position and its reasoning
The majority (جمهور) say it is not obligatory because the loss of purpose (تفويت الغرض) comes from the servant's own act of disobedience, not from God's act of keeping alive.
Note the phrase: 'لأن تفويت الغرض إنما هو بفعل العبد'.
3
Examine the Mu'tazila counterargument
Some Mu'tazila argue that if God knows sin will result, then not ending the life leads to loss of purpose after its attainment, which is evil; thus it is obligatory.
Contrast with the majority: they see the loss as caused by the servant, not by God's inaction.
4
Consider the examples and refutations
The text gives examples: God did not cut short those who later disbelieved, nor Iblis, and refutes the interpretation of Quranic verse about Iblis. It also clarifies when cutting short is NOT obligatory even for repentant sinners.
The example of Iblis is particularly important in refuting the Mu'tazila claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (الرابع الاحترام) إذا علم من المعصوم أو التائب أنه يكفر أو يفسق لو بقي لما في تركه من تفويت الغرض، وجمهورهم على أنه لا يجب لأن التفويت إنما هو بفعل العبد. ذهب بعض المعتزلة إلى أن الباري تعالى إذا علم من المؤمن المعصوم أو التائب أنه إن أبقاه حيّاً يكفر أو يفسق يجب اخترامه لأن في تركه تفويتاً للغرض بعد حصوله وهو قبيح. والأكثرون على أنه لا يجب، لأن تفويت الغرض، إنما هو بفعل العبد، وهو المعصية لا بالتبقية ، ولأنه لم يخترم من كفر بعد الإيمان، وعصى بعد الطاعة، ولم يخترم إبليس مع ما روي أنه عَبَدَ الله تعالى عشرين ألف سنة ثم كفر. والقول بأن ذلك كان مع النفاق بعيد جداً ، والاستدلال بقوله تعالى وكان من الكافرين (۲) ضعيف لقول المفسرين إنه بمعنى صار أو كان من جنس كفرة الجن وشياطينهم ، أو كان في علم الله تعالى ممن يكفر، وأما إذا علم من المؤمن أنه يكفر أو يفسق ثم يتوب، أو من الكافر والفاسق أنه يزداد كفراً وعصياناً، ولا يتوب فلا يجب الاخترام كما لا يجب تبقية المؤمن إذا علم منه زيادة الطاعة، ولا تبقية الطفل إذا علم منه أنه لو كلفه ،آمن، وأما تبقية إبليس وتمكينه . فقال أبو علي : إنما يحسن إذا كان المعلوم أن من يعصي بوسوسته يعصي لولا وسوسته
(١) الوسوسة : حديث النفس يقال : وسوست إليه نفسه (وسوسة) وسواساً بكسر الواو. والوسواس : بالفتح الاسم كالزلزال والزلزال وقوله تعالى ي فوسوس لهما الشيطان . يريد إليهما، ولكن العرب توصل بهذه الحروف كلها الفعل ويقال لصوت الحلى (وسواس). والوسواس أيضاً اسم الشيطان
He said: 'The fourth is al-ikhtiram (cutting short).' If it is known from an infallible or repentant person that he would disbelieve or sin if he remained, because in leaving him there is a loss of the purpose, and the majority hold that it is not obligatory because the loss is only by the servant's act. Some Mu'tazila held that if God knows of the believing infallible or repentant that if He keeps him alive he would disbelieve or sin, then cutting him short is obligatory, because in leaving him there is a loss of purpose after its attainment, and that is evil. But the majority hold it is not obligatory, because the loss of purpose is only by the servant's act, which is disobedience, not by keeping alive, and because He did not cut short the lives of those who disbelieved after faith and sinned after obedience, and He did not cut short Iblis despite what is narrated that he worshipped Allah for twenty thousand years then disbelieved. And the claim that that was with hypocrisy is very far-fetched, and the argument with His statement 'and he was among the disbelievers' is weak because the commentators say it means 'he became' or 'he was of the genus of the disbelievers among the jinn and their devils', or he was in God's knowledge among those who would disbelieve. And if He knows of the believer that he will disbelieve or sin and then repent, or of the disbeliever and sinner that he will increase in disbelief and sin and not repent, then cutting short is not obligatory, just as keeping the believer alive is not obligatory if He knows of him increase in obedience, and keeping the child alive is not obligatory if He knows that if He tasked him he would believe. As for keeping Iblis alive and empowering him, Abu Ali said: It is only good if it is known that those who sin through his whispering would sin even without his whispering. (Footnote: Al-waswasa: whispering of the self. It is said: 'waswasa to him his nafs (waswasa) waswasan with kasr of the waw. And al-waswas with fat-h: the noun like zalzal and zalzal. And His statement 'then Satan whispered to them' means 'to them', but the Arabs connect with all these letters the verb. And it is said for the sound of jewelry (waswas). And al-waswas is also the name of Satan.)
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This is a detailed discussion on the fourth principle: al-ikhtiram (cutting short life). It presents the views of the majority (جمهور) and some Mu'tazila. The majority hold that God is not obligated to end the life of someone He knows will sin, because the loss of purpose (تفويت الغرض) is caused by the servant's own act of disobedience, not by God's act of keeping him alive. They cite examples: God did not cut short those who later disbelieved after faith, nor did He cut short Iblis despite his long worship and subsequent disbelief. The text then refutes the interpretation of 'and he was among the disbelievers' regarding Iblis, citing commentator opinions. It further distinguishes cases: If God knows a believer will sin but then repent, or a sinner will increase in sin without repenting, then cutting short is not obligatory. Finally, it mentions Abu Ali's view on keeping Iblis alive: it is only good if those who sin through his whispering would sin anyway.

Key Terms
الاحترام
المعصوم
التائب
تفويت الغرض
الجمهور
المعتزلة
إبليس
الوسوسة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's knowledge of future sin obligates Him to prevent it by ending the person's life.
Correction: The majority hold that the sin is the servant's own act, so God is not obligated to end life; the loss of purpose is due to the servant's disobedience, not God's inaction.

Checkpoint
Why do the majority hold that God is not obligated to cut short the life of someone He knows will sin?
A) Because God does not know the future.
B) Because the loss of purpose is caused by the servant's act, not by God's keeping him alive.
C) Because sin is predetermined by God.
D) Because the Mu'tazila disagreed.

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge of future sin does not compel Him to end life; the responsibility for sin lies with the servant.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that your sins are solely your own fault, or do you secretly blame God for not intervening? How does this lesson's argument that God's foreknowledge does not obligate Him to stop you challenge your excuses? What will you change today to own your choices fully?
?
Iblis worshipped for 20,000 years and then fell. Is there any area of your faith where you are relying on past devotion instead of present vigilance? What would it mean for you to live today as if your next sin could undo years of obedience?
?
If God does not cut short your life to prevent sin, what does that reveal about the weight He places on your free will? Are you willing to accept that your eternal destiny is shaped by your decisions, not His intervention? How will you live differently knowing that no external force will rescue you from your own choices?

The Mu'tazila Doctrine of al-Aslah and Its Refutation
Exposition of al-aslah and its critique.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila doctrine of al-aslah (the most beneficial) and the Sunni theological critique that shows it leads to absurd and contradictory conclusions.

Core Idea
If God must always do the best, then even Hell becomes a favor.

The Mu'tazila argued that God is obligated to do what is most beneficial for His creatures. Sa'd al-Din al-Taftazani presents this view and then subjects it to a devastating critique, showing that it leads to logical absurdities that contradict scriptural and rational principles. This lesson unpacks both the claim and the response.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
The passage is cut off at the end, but the main argument is complete.
2
Understand the Mu'tazila position
Identify the two Mu'tazila sub-schools (Basrans and Baghdadans) and their agreement on the obligation of al-aslah, including the idea that God must do the utmost possible for everyone.
Note the terms 'al-Basriyya' and 'al-Baghdadiyya'.
3
Analyze the analogies
The Mu'tazila use analogies of a wise commander and a generous host. Reflect on how these analogies are meant to support the obligation of al-aslah.
Consider why these analogies might be flawed.
4
Grasp the critique
The text then presents a series of absurd consequences that would follow if al-aslah were obligatory. Identify each consequence and see how it contradicts observable reality or God's attributes.
The critique uses reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (الخامس الأصلح) الخامس الأصلح للعباد في الدين عند البصرية، والدنيا أيضاً عند البغدادية، واتفقوا على وجوب الاقدار والتمكين وأقصى ما يمكن من الأصلح لكل أحد، حتى ليس في المقدور ما لو فعل بالكفار لآمنوا جميعاً، وإلا لكان تركه بخلاً وسفهاً، كالحكيم (٢) أمر بطاعته، ولم يعط مع القدرة وعدم التضرر ما يوصل إليه، وكالكريم استدعى حضور ضيف، وترك تلقيه بالبشاشة إلى الفظاظة (۳) وقد يتمسك بأن وجوب الفعل عند خلوص الداعي، والقدرة قطعي، ونحن نقول بعد التنزل، لو وجب الأصلح لما خلق الكافر الفقير المبتلى طول عمره بالمحن والآفات لوجب بمقتضى تمثيلاتكم على كل أحد ما هو الأصلح لعبيده، وألزم أن يكون الأصلح للكفار الخلود في النار، وأن يكون كل ما يفعله بالعباد أداء الواجب، فلا يستوجب شكراً، وأن تتناهى مقدوراته من اللطف، وأن تكون إماتة الأنبياء، والأولياء والمحسنين ،والكرماء، وتبقية الظلمة ،والغواة، وإبليس والذرايات ومن
He said: 'The fifth is al-aslah (the most beneficial).' The fifth: the most beneficial for servants in religion according to the Basrans, and also in worldly matters according to the Baghdadans. And they agreed on the obligation of decree and empowerment and the utmost possible of what is most beneficial for everyone, such that there is nothing within power which, if done to the disbelievers, they would all believe; otherwise, leaving it would be miserly and foolish, like a wise man commanding obedience to Him and not giving, despite ability and absence of harm, what leads to it, and like a generous man inviting a guest and then meeting him with frowning instead of cheerfulness. And it may be argued that the obligation of an act when the motive is unalloyed and ability are certain. And we say, after concession: If the most beneficial were obligatory, then why did He create the poor disbeliever afflicted throughout his life with trials and afflictions? It would be necessary, according to your analogies, that everyone should receive what is most beneficial for him, and it would entail that the most beneficial for disbelievers is eternal damnation, and that everything He does for servants is fulfilling an obligation, so He deserves no gratitude, and that His abilities of grace would be finite, and that the deaths of prophets, awliya', good people, generous people, and the survival of oppressors, misleaders, Iblis, the particles, and...
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth principle: al-aslah (the most beneficial). It outlines the Mu'tazila position: the Basrans say it is obligatory in religious matters, the Baghdadans also in worldly matters. They agree that God must provide the best possible for every individual, to the extent that if He had done something to make all disbelievers believe, He would have done it; otherwise His omission would be stingy and foolish. They give analogies of a wise commander and a generous host. The text then presents a critique: if al-aslah were obligatory, why did God create the poor disbeliever always afflicted? It would entail absurd consequences: the most beneficial for disbelievers would be eternal fire, all God's acts would be obligatory duties, so no gratitude is owed, God's abilities of grace would be limited, and the deaths of good people and survival of evil people would be problematic. The passage is incomplete.

Key Terms

البصرية
البغدادية
الإقدار
التمكين
اللطف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the Mu'tazila require God to always give the maximum benefit to everyone, and that this is a reasonable expectation.
Correction: The critique shows this leads to irrational consequences such as Hell being a benefit and God not deserving gratitude, proving the doctrine cannot be true.

Checkpoint
According to the critique, what is an absurd consequence of the obligation of al-aslah?
A) That God would have no power.
B) That the most beneficial for disbelievers would be eternal damnation.
C) That God would not be generous.
D) That everyone would be a believer.

Key Takeaway
The doctrine of al-aslah is rationally untenable because it leads to conclusions that contradict God's wisdom, justice, and observable reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When life doesn't go as you hoped, do you ever feel that God has failed to give you the best? How does this lesson's critique of al-aslah challenge that feeling? Will you consciously remind yourself that God's wisdom may involve trials that are ultimately for your good, even if not 'best' by your measure?
?
The Mu'tazila claimed that God must do the most beneficial to be just. But the refutation shows that if all God's acts are obligatory, He deserves no gratitude. Examine your own heart: do you sometimes act as if God owes you blessings? When you pray and receive, do you thank Him freely, or do you feel entitled? What would change if you saw every blessing as a gift, not a duty?
?
One absurd consequence listed is that if al-aslah were obligatory, then eternal damnation would be the 'best' for disbelievers. This reveals the danger of imposing human logic on divine justice. Are there aspects of your understanding of God's justice that might unwittingly confine Him to your own reasoning? Will you submit to His revelation even when it seems paradoxical to your mind?
