The Mu'tazilite Doctrine of al-Aslah
Introduces the doctrine and its proof via analogies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite claim that God is obligated to do what is most beneficial for servants, both in religion and this world.

Core Idea
Mu'tazilites argued God must always do the best or be considered miserly.

This lesson introduces the Mu'tazilite doctrine of al-aslah, which asserts that God is morally obliged to create the most beneficial circumstances for every person. The passage distinguishes the Basran and Baghdadi views and presents their core argument and analogies.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the passage once to get the overall structure: statement of doctrine, then analogies, then proof.
2
Identify the three elements
Notice (1) the definition of al-aslah, (2) the two analogies from human behavior, and (3) the beginning of their rational argument.
The analogies illustrate why they think God must provide means to obey.
3
Reflect on the claim
Ask yourself: What does it imply about God's nature if He is obligated like a human host?
This sets up the refutation in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (الخامس الأصلح) الخامس الأصلح للعباد في الدين عند البصرية، والدنيا أيضاً عند البغدادية، واتفقوا على وجوب الاقدار والتمكين وأقصى ما يمكن من الأصلح لكل أحد، حتى ليس في المقدور ما لو فعل بالكفار لآمنوا جميعاً، وإلا لكان تركه بخلاً وسفهاً، كالحكيم أمر بطاعته، ولم يعط مع القدرة وعدم التضرر ما يوصل إليه، وكالكريم استدعى حضور ضيف، وترك تلقيه بالبشاشة إلى الفظاظة وقد يتمسك بأن وجوب الفعل عند خلوص الداعي، والقدرة قطعي، ونحن نقول بعد التنزل، لو وجب الأصلح لما خلق الكافر الفقير المبتلى طول عمره بالمحن والآفات لوجب بمقتضى تمثيلاتكم على كل أحد ما هو الأصلح لعبيده، وألزم أن يكون الأصلح للكفار الخلود في النار، وأن يكون كل ما يفعله بالعباد أداء الواجب، فلا يستوجب شكراً، وأن تتناهى مقدوراته من اللطف، وأن تكون إماتة الأنبياء، والأولياء والمحسنين ،والكرماء، وتبقية الظلمة ،والغواة، وإبليس والذرايات ومن علم منهم الارتداد، وتصلح للعباد وأن لا يحسن الدعاء لدفع البلاء، وأن يتساوى امتنانه على الكفر وعلى الانبياء، وأن لا يبقى له في التفضل مجال، ولا تكون له خيرة في الأفضال .
He said: (The fifth is al-aslah). The fifth: the most beneficial for servants in religion is according to the Basrans, and also in the world according to the Baghdadis. They agreed on the obligation of decrees and enabling, and the utmost possible of al-aslah for everyone, such that there is nothing in [God's] power that if He did to the disbelievers they would all believe; otherwise His leaving it would be miserliness and foolishness. Like a wise person who commands obedience to him, but does not give, while having ability and no harm, what leads to it. And like a generous person who invites a guest, but treats him with harshness instead of cheerfulness. And there may also be an argument that the obligation of an act when the motive and ability are pure is certain. And we say, after conceding: if al-aslah were obligatory, then [God] would not have created the poor disbeliever afflicted all his life with trials and calamities, for according to your analogies it would be necessary that what is most beneficial for His servants [be done] for everyone. And it would necessitate that al-aslah for the disbelievers is eternal fire, and that everything He does to servants is fulfilling an obligation so He deserves no gratitude, and that His capacities for kindness are finite, and that the death of prophets, saints, benefactors, and generous people, and the survival of oppressors, wrongdoers, Iblis, and vermin, and those from whom apostasy is known, and that there is no goodness in prayer for removal of calamity, and that His favor upon disbelievers and upon prophets is equal, and that there remains no room for supererogatory favor, and He has no choice in bestowing excellence.
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This passage is a critical summary of the Mu'tazilite doctrine of al-aslah (the most beneficial) and its refutation. It begins by stating the doctrine: the Basrans say God must do what is most beneficial in religion, while the Baghdadis extend it to this world as well. They agree that God's decrees and enabling are obligatory, and that He does the utmost possible good for each person, including not doing a kindness that would make all disbelievers believe, otherwise He would be miserly. Two analogies from human behavior are given: a wise commander who fails to provide means to obey, and a generous host who receives a guest harshly. The author then responds by conceding the argument for the sake of argument and listing the necessary consequences if al-aslah were truly obligatory. These include: that disbelievers should stay in fire eternally (since that is 'best' for them?), that all God's acts become obligatory duties so He deserves no thanks, that His capacity for kindness is limited, that prophets and saints would die while wrongdoers survive, that prayer for relief is useless, and that God's favor to believers and disbelievers would be equal. These consequences are absurd, thus the original premise is false. The passage uses reductio ad absurdum to refute the Mu'tazilite position.

Key Terms
أصلح
الاقدار
التمكين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mu'tazilites only care about worldly benefit, not religion.
Correction: The passage clearly states that for Basrans the obligation is in religion; Baghdadis include this world as well, but religion is primary.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what did the Basrans and Baghdadis disagree on?
A) Whether God exists
B) Whether al-aslah applies to this world as well as religion
C) Whether God can be miserly
D) Whether disbelievers can become believers

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite doctrine of al-aslah claims God must do the most beneficial for every person, using analogies from human conduct to argue that any less would be miserly.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation where you feel God has not given you the best, do you quietly accuse Him of being less than generous? How does the Mu'tazilite argument force you to examine that feeling against the backdrop of God's absolute wisdom? Will you repent of that subtle ingratitude today?
?
Imagine if you could rearrange the world so that every person believed—would you do it? If not, what does that reveal about your own understanding of wisdom and justice? Are you willing to submit to God's choices even when they seem less than 'best' to you?
?
Do you ever treat those you love—your spouse, your children, your friends—with the same logic: that you must give them the best or you are stingy? How does that human analogy actually expose your own expectations of God? Will you let go of demanding God fit your standard of 'best' and instead trust His perfect plan?

The Absurd Consequences of al-Aslah
Lists absurd consequences if al-aslah were obligatory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the reductio ad absurdum refutation of the Mu'tazilite doctrine of al-aslah through its impossible implications.

Core Idea
If God were obligated to do the best, it would lead to absurdities that contradict basic Islamic beliefs.

The author now responds to the Mu'tazilite claim by temporarily conceding it and then showing the necessary consequences. These consequences are so absurd—ranging from God deserving no gratitude to the eternity of disbelievers in fire—that the original doctrine must be false.

Guided Path
1
Read the list of consequences
Read through the Arabic and translation carefully, noting each absurd consequence the author lists.
There are about 10 distinct implications.
2
Identify the method
Recognize that this is a reductio ad absurdum: the author assumes the doctrine for argument's sake and shows it leads to false conclusions.
3
Reflect on the theological impact
Think about how each consequence contradicts core Islamic teachings about God's mercy, justice, and voluntary generosity.
For example, if everything God does is a duty, He deserves no gratitude—contrary to the Qur'an.

Source Text

The Mu'tazilite Analogy and Its Refutation
Mu'tazilite analogies for al-aslah with Ash'arite response start.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite proof by analogy for al-aslah and why the Ash'arites reject it by pointing to God's self-sufficiency.

Core Idea
God's self-sufficiency breaks the analogy from human command.

The Mu'tazila argue that a wise commander must provide the means to obey, so God must do the same. The Ash'arite response begins by highlighting that the human commander needs obedience, whereas God does not. This lesson unpacks that argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Notice how the Mu'tazila present multiple analogies to support their case.
The response is cut off at the end of the page.
2
Identify the three analogies
Find the three scenarios: the wise commander, the reconciliation with an enemy, and the host and guest. Notice the pattern: each involves a wise or considerate person who must act to achieve a goal.
These analogies are meant to show that God, being wise, must provide the means to obedience.
3
Examine the Ash'arite response
The response begins with 'قلنا' (We said). It concedes that the command necessitates will only in a wise person in need of obedience. Highlight the word 'محتاج' (needy). This is the key point.
The response is incomplete, but the direction is clear: the analogy only works if God needs our obedience, which He does not.
4
Reflect on why neediness matters
Ask yourself: if the commander does not need obedience, is he blameworthy for not providing means? The Ash'arite answer is no. This undermines the Mu'tazilite proof.
This connects to the broader doctrine of God's self-sufficiency (al-ghina).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا: نحن نقطع بأن الحكيم إذا أمر بطاعته وقدر على أن يعطي المأمور ما يصل به إلى الطاعة من غير تضرر بذلك ، ثم لم يفعل كان مذموماً عند العقلاء، معدوداً في زمرة البخلاء، ولذلك من دعى عدوه إلى الموالاة، والرجوع إلى الطاعة، لا يجوز أن يعامله من الغلظ واللين إلا بما هو أنجع في حصول المراد، وأدعى إلى ترك العناد، وأيضاً من اتخذ ضيافة لرجل واستدعى حضوره، وعلم أنه لو تلقاه ببشر وطلاقة وجه دخل وأكل وإلا فلا. فالواجب عليه البشر والطلاقة والملاطفة لا أضدادها . قلنا: ذاك بعد تسليم استلزام الأمر الإرادة إنما هو في حكيم محتاج إلى طاعة
They said: We are certain that if a wise person commands obedience to himself and is able to give the commanded person what would make him reach obedience without harm from that, then did not do it, he would be blameworthy among rational people, counted among the miserly. Similarly, if someone invites his enemy to friendship and return to obedience, he should not treat him with harshness or softness except with what is most effective in achieving the goal and more conducive to abandoning stubbornness. Also, if someone hosts a meal for a man and invites him, and knows that if he receives him with a cheerful and smiling face he will enter and eat, otherwise not, then he must be cheerful, smiling, and pleasant, not the opposite. We said: That [is] after conceding that the command necessitates will only in the case of a wise person in need of obedience.
Source pages p. 330

Commentary
This passage contains the Mu'tazilite proof for al-aslah and the beginning of the Ash'arite refutation. The Mu'tazila argue by analogy from human experience: a wise commander who commands obedience but fails to provide the means would be blameworthy and miserly. Similarly, someone seeking reconciliation with an enemy must use effective means, and a host must welcome a guest warmly. Thus, God, as the wisest, must provide the means to obedience. The response begins: 'We said: That [is] after conceding that the command necessitates will only in the case of a wise person in need of obedience.' This refutes the analogy by pointing out that the human examples involve a commander who needs compliance, whereas God is self-sufficient and does not need our obedience. The response is cut off but sets up the key distinction: God's self-sufficiency breaks the analogy.

Key Terms
الحكيم
المأمور
البشر
الطلاقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the analogy is valid for God because He is wise, without considering whether He needs obedience.
Correction: The Ash'arite response shows that the analogy only applies to a commander who needs obedience; God is self-sufficient and has no need, so the analogy does not hold.

Checkpoint
Why does the response reject the Mu'tazilite analogy about the wise commander?
A) Because God is not wise
B) Because the commander in the analogy needs obedience, but God does not
C) Because the commander is not real
D) Because the commanded person cannot obey

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite argument from analogy fails because it assumes God needs our obedience, but He is absolutely self-sufficient.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you obey God, do you ever feel that He now owes you something in return? How does the truth that God is not in need of your obedience change that expectation? Commit today to worship Him with no strings attached.
?
Have you ever found yourself angry at God because He didn't give you what you thought was 'best'? This lesson shows that your standards of wisdom are not God's. Will you hand over your definition of 'best' to Him and trust His choice?
?
The Mu'tazila assumed God must act like a wise human. In what area of your life have you been subconsciously applying that assumption—expecting God to behave as you would? Repent and ask for the grace to accept His different ways.

The Fourth Argument: No Thanks for Duty
Sanusi presents the fourth argument against al-aslah: no gratitude for obligatory acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why obligating God to do the most beneficial would eliminate any basis for gratitude toward Him, and how this argument challenges the concept of al-aslah.

Core Idea
If the most beneficial were obligatory, God's acts would be duties, not gifts — no thanks required.

The fourth argument against the obligation of al-aslah draws on a simple moral intuition: we do not thank someone for merely doing what they are required to do. Sanusi applies this to God: if He were obligated to create the most beneficial, His acts would be like returning a deposit — necessary, not generous. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Read the Arabic sentence for the fourth argument and its translation. Notice the analogy (returning a deposit/debt).
The argument is very concise; the analogy carries the weight.
2
Identify the underlying principle
Ask: What principle about gratitude and obligation does this argument rely on? (We thank for free favor, not required duty.)
3
Apply to the broader debate
Consider: If God's actions are always the most beneficial as a duty, can any action be a free gift deserving thanks? This undermines the basis for gratitude in religion.
The argument is a reductio ad absurdum of the obligation view.

Source Text

الرابع : يلزم أن لا يستوجب الله على فعل شكراً لكونه مؤدياً للواجب كمن يرد. وديعة (١)، وديناً لازماً. الخامس : مقدورات الله تعالى غير متناهية فأي قدر يضبطونه (۲) في الأصلح فالمزيد عليه ممكن، فيجب لا إلى حد . فإن قيل: ربما يصير ضم المزيد إليه ،مفسدة، كما أن ضم النافع إلى النافع يصير مضرة، فيما إذا زاد من الدواء (۳) على القدر الذي فيه الشفاء . أجيب بأنه لا يعقل أن يكون ضم الصلاح إلى الصلاح فساداً، وتقدر قدر من الدواء للشفاء إنما هو بطريق جري (٤) العادة من الله تعالى، فإنه النافع والضار لا الدواء، حتى لو غير العادة، وجعل الشفاء في القدر الزائد جاز. ولو سلّم فالنفع مقدور والزيادة في الدواء ليس من ضم النفع إلى النفع، بل من ضم ليس ينفع مثلاً لنافع في الحمى قدر من المبرد يقاوم الحرارة الغالية، فإذ زيد عليه قدر فليس ينفع ، لأنه عمله ليس في دفع تلك الحرارة التي هي " المرض، بل في إثبات برودة تزيل الصحة والاعتدال (٥) بخلاف الصلاح في الدين، فإنه لا يتقدر بقدر، ولا ينتهي إلى حد وكل صلاح ضم إلى صلاح يكون أصلح . فإن قيل : يتقدر الأصلح لا لتناهي قدرة الله تعالى. بل لما علم أن المزيد عليه ينصير سبباً (٦) للطغيان .
Fourth: It would follow that God does not deserve thanks for an act because He is performing an obligatory duty, like one who returns a deposit and a necessary debt. Fifth: The possibilities of God Almighty are infinite, so what measure do they fix for the most beneficial? Addition is possible, so it must be without limit. If it is said: Perhaps adding to it becomes corruption, just as adding benefit to benefit becomes harm, in the case of increasing the medicine beyond the amount that brings healing. It is answered: It is inconceivable that adding benefit to benefit be corruption. The determination of the amount of medicine for healing is only by way of the customary practice of God Almighty, for He is the Benefiter and the Harmer, not the medicine, so that even if He changed the custom and made healing in the increased amount, it would be permissible. And even if conceded, benefit is within power, and the increase in medicine is not adding benefit to benefit, but rather adding something that is not beneficial – for example, for a fever patient, a certain amount of coolant counteracts the intense heat; if one increases the amount, it does not benefit, because its action is not to repel that heat which is the illness, but to produce coldness that removes health and balance – unlike benefit in religion, for it is not limited by a measure and does not end at a limit, and every benefit added to benefit is more beneficial. If it is said: The most beneficial is limited not due to the finitude of God's power, but because He knows that adding to it would become a cause of transgression.
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth and fifth arguments against the obligation of al-aslah, along with objections and answers. The Fourth Argument: If God were obligated to do the most beneficial, He would not deserve thanks for any act, since it would be merely fulfilling a duty, like returning a deposit or paying a debt – acts that do not merit praise. The Fifth Argument: God's power is infinite, so any specific measure of 'most beneficial' would be arbitrary; one could always add more benefit, so it would have to be infinite, which is impossible. An objection compares adding benefit to adding medicine beyond the healing dose, which causes harm. The answer is twofold: (a) Adding benefit to benefit is not conceptually harmful; medicine works by God's custom, and God could make the extra dose healing. (b) Even if conceded, the medicine example is different because the extra dose is not beneficial – e.g., too much coolant harmfully lowers temperature – whereas spiritual benefit has no such limit; adding benefit always increases benefit. A further objection says that al-aslah is limited not by power but because more would cause transgression. The passage ends with this objection, leaving it unanswered in the excerpt.

Key Terms
الأصلح (al-aṣlaḥ)
الشكر (al-shukr)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that God is always deserving of thanks regardless of obligation, but this argument shows that if obligation exists, gratitude loses its meaning.
Correction: Sanusi argues that gratitude is only for free favors, not required duties.

Checkpoint
According to the fourth argument, why would God not deserve thanks if al-aslah were obligatory?
A) Because God is too great to be thanked
B) Because His acts would be like returning a deposit or paying a debt — mere duty
C) Because humans are unable to thank Him properly
D) Because thanks is only for beneficial acts, and all acts are beneficial

Key Takeaway
The fourth argument asserts that obligating God to do the most beneficial would cancel the basis for gratitude, since thanks is only for free favor, not required duty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever catch yourself feeling entitled to God's blessings, as if He owes you something? How does Sanusi's argument reframe what you think you deserve from Him?
?
When was the last time you sincerely thanked God for something you considered 'ordinary' or 'obligatory' (like waking up healthy)? If God were only doing His duty, would that thanks still carry weight?
?
If you truly believed every good thing you have is an unearned gift, not a right, what would change about how you ask God for more?

The Fifth Argument: Infinite Possibilities and Benefit Without Limit
Sanusi presents the fifth argument, an objection, a detailed answer, and a final objection about transgression.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the infinity of God's power makes a fixed 'most beneficial' impossible, and why adding spiritual benefit never becomes harmful, unlike material benefit.

Core Idea
God's power is infinite, so any fixed best is impossible; spiritual benefit has no saturation point.

The fifth argument presses on the infinity of God's creative possibilities. If God can always do more, then any specific 'most beneficial' would be arbitrary — we could always add more. The objector tries to show that more can become harmful, like medicine overdose. Sanusi responds by distinguishing material from spiritual benefit, and further answers an objection about transgression. This lesson walks through the exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the entire fifth argument passage
Read the Arabic and translation from 'الخامس' to the end. Notice the objection, answer, and second objection.
The passage includes three moves: argument, objection, reply, and a second objection (unanswered here).
2
Understand the core philosophical problem
The problem: If God's power is unlimited, any finite measure of benefit can be exceeded. So there cannot be a 'most beneficial' state; it would require an infinite series, which is impossible for a finite world.
3
Analyze the medicine analogy
The objector says that adding benefit can become harm (like too much medicine). The author gives a two-part answer: (a) medicine works by God's custom, and God could change it; (b) even accepting the analogy, extra medicine is not beneficial because it disrupts balance, unlike spiritual benefit which is always beneficial when added.
This is the key distinction between material and spiritual benefit.
4
Examine the transgression objection
The second objection says that more benefit could cause transgression in people. The passage ends there — consider how this objection relates to the argument.
This objection relies on human psychology, not the inherent nature of benefit.

The Fourth Objection: Obligation Excludes Gratitude
The author raises an objection that God cannot be thanked for fulfilling a duty like returning a deposit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth objection against the Mu'tazilite view that God must do the most beneficial, which argues that if God's actions are obligatory, they do not merit gratitude.

Core Idea
If God is obligated to act, His actions are like a debt being paid — not a gift to be thanked for.

The Mu'tazilites held that God is obliged to do what is best for creation. But this raises a problem: If God is acting under obligation, can He be thanked? The fourth objection argues that an obligated act is like returning a deposit — it is a duty, not a favor, and thus does not deserve gratitude. Let's see the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic sentence and its translation carefully. Notice that it presents the fourth objection in a condensed form.
The passage uses a simple analogy.
2
Understand the analogy
The analogy compares God's obligated act to returning a deposit or paying a debt. Both are obligatory acts that do not call for gratitude. Ponder how this undermines attributing generosity to God if His actions are necessitated.
3
Reflect on the broader impact
This objection challenges the very concept of divine benevolence if God's actions are not voluntary favors but required duties. Think about how this affects the Mu'tazilite position.
This objection is part of a series of arguments against wujub al-aslah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : يلزم أن لا يستوجب الله على فعل شكراً لكونه مؤدياً للواجب كمن يرد وديعة (١)، وديناً لازماً.
The fourth: It necessarily follows that God does not deserve gratitude for an act because it is a fulfillment of an obligation, like one who returns a deposit and a necessary debt.
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth objection against the Mu'tazilite theory that God must do the most beneficial. The objection states: If God's actions are obligatory (wājib), then He does not deserve gratitude (shukr) for them, just as a person who returns a deposit or pays a debt does not deserve thanks because they are merely fulfilling an obligation. Thus, the concept of God being thanked for His goodness is undermined if His actions are necessitated.

Key Terms
يستوجب
شكر
مؤدياً للواجب
وديعة
دين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection denies that God deserves thanks for any action whatsoever.
Correction: The objection only applies if God's actions are necessitated by obligation. It does not deny gratitude for voluntary, non-obligatory acts.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the fourth objection?
A) God always deserves gratitude for any act.
B) If God's acts are obligatory, then He does not deserve gratitude for them.
C) Returning a deposit is a praiseworthy act.
D) The Mu'tazilites believe God is not generous.

Key Takeaway
The fourth objection shows that if God's actions are necessitated by obligation, they become like duties, not favors, undermining the basis for gratitude.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you thank Allah for His blessings, do you truly see them as voluntary favors, or do you sometimes feel He 'owes' you because you've been obedient? How does this lesson challenge that assumption? Will you repent for any sense of entitlement?
?
Consider a time you expected gratitude from someone for doing what was required of you. Does that expectation reflect a misunderstanding of obligation and favor? How can you apply this lesson to your relationship with Allah's commands?

The Fifth Objection: God's Infinite Power and the Bounds of al-Aslah
The author argues against the objection that God's infinite power makes al-aslah unbounded.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth objection against the requirement of al-aslah and the author's response that distinguishes between material benefit and religious benefit.

Core Idea
God's infinite power does not make al-aslah unbounded; religious benefit has no limit.

This passage presents a critical objection to the aslah theory: if God's power is infinite, why should al-aslah be limited? The author answers by clarifying that adding benefit to benefit cannot become harm, unlike material things like medicine. He also addresses the concern that increasing benefit might cause tyranny, arguing that the opponent's position is inconsistent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, noting the flow of objection and response.
Look for the key terms: مقدورات, غير متناهية, أصلح.
2
Identify the structure
Break down the passage into: objection (line 1), first answer (lines 2-5), counter-objection (lines 5-6), final answer (lines 6-10).
3
Analyze the medicine analogy
Understand why the author rejects the analogy: medicine works by God's custom and adding more does not add benefit, unlike religious benefit.
Religious benefit is not limited by measure.
4
Examine the final objection about tyranny
See how the opponent claims limitation to avoid tyranny, and how the author responds by noting inconsistency.
The opponent's compensation doctrine is self-contradictory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس : مقدورات الله تعالى غير متناهية فأي قدر يضبطونه (۲) في الأصلح فالمزيد عليه ممكن، فيجب لا إلى حد . فإن قيل: ربما يصير ضم المزيد إليه مفسدة، كما أن ضم النافع إلى النافع يصير مضرة، فيما إذا زاد من الدواء (۳) على القدر الذي فيه الشفاء . أجيب بأنه لا يعقل أن يكون ضم الصلاح إلى الصلاح فساداً، وتقدر قدر من الدواء للشفاء إنما هو بطريق جري (٤) العادة من الله تعالى، فإنه النافع والضار لا الدواء، حتى لو غير العادة، وجعل الشفاء في القدر الزائد جاز. ولو سلّم فالنفع مقدور والزيادة في الدواء ليس من ضم النفع إلى النفع، بل من ضم ليس ينفع مثلاً لنافع في الحمى قدر من المبرد يقاوم الحرارة الغالية، فإذ زيد عليه قدر فليس ينفع ، لأنه عمله ليس في دفع تلك الحرارة التي هي "المرض"، بل في إثبات برودة تزيل الصحة والاعتدال (٥) بخلاف الصلاح في الدين، فإنه لا يتقدر بقدر، ولا ينتهي إلى حد وكل صلاح ضم إلى صلاح يكون أصلح . فإن قيل : يتقدر الأصلح لا لتناهي قدرة الله تعالى. بل لما علم أن المزيد عليه ينصير سبباً (٦) للطغيان . أجيب : بأنكم لا تعتبرون في وجوب الأصلح جانب المعلوم حيث تزعمون أن من علم الله تعالى أنه لو كلفه طغى وعصى واستكبر وكفر يجب على الله تعويضه علمه بأنه لا يدركه بل يقع في العقاب ولو أنه اخترمه قبل كمال العقل للثواب مع خلص نجياً.
The fifth: The things within the power of God Almighty are infinite. So whatever measure they fix for al-aslah, an increase upon it is possible, so it must be without limit. For it is said: Perhaps adding more to it would become a corruption, just as adding beneficial to beneficial becomes harmful, as when one increases the medicine beyond the amount that brings cure. It is answered: It is inconceivable that adding benefit to benefit would be corruption. The determination of the amount of medicine for cure is only by way of the running of the custom of God Almighty; indeed He is the Benefiter and the Harmer, not the medicine. Even if He changed the custom and made the cure in the extra amount, it would be permissible. And if it is granted, then benefit is within power, and the increase in medicine is not from adding benefit to benefit, but rather from adding something that does not benefit; for example, for a beneficial [medicine] in fever, a certain amount of coolant that counteracts the intense heat; if one increases the amount, it does not benefit, because its action is not in repelling that heat which is the illness, but in establishing coldness that removes health and balance, unlike religious benefit, which is not limited by a measure and does not reach a limit, and every benefit added to benefit is more beneficial. For it is said: Al-aslah is limited not because of the infinity of God's power, but because it is known that increasing it would become a cause of tyranny. It is answered: You do not consider in the obligation of al-aslah the aspect of what is known, when you claim that for one whom God knows that if He burdened him he would transgress, disobey, become arrogant, and disbelieve, God must compensate him with the knowledge that he will not attain it but will fall into punishment, even if He had terminated him before the perfection of intellect for reward with pure salvation.
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth objection and its detailed answer. The objection argues: Since God's power (maqdūrāt) is infinite, any limit set for what is most beneficial (al-aslah) can be increased, so it must be unbounded. The opponent's reply: Adding more might cause corruption, like adding more medicine beyond the curative dose causes harm. The author answers: It is inconceivable that adding benefit to benefit would corrupt; medicine works only by God's custom; God can change it. Even if we accept the analogy, the increase in medicine is not adding benefit to benefit but adding something non-beneficial. Religious benefit does not have a limit. Then a further objection: The limitation is not due to infinite power but because increasing it would cause tyranny. The author responds: The opponent inconsistently ignores the aspect of knowledge—they claim that God must compensate one He knows would reject, but that compensation ends in punishment, which is contradictory.

غير متناهية
أصلح
مفسدة
المزيد
جري العادة
الشفاء
الصلاح في الدين
الطغيان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Adding more benefit always leads to harm, just as too much medicine is harmful.
Correction: The author argues that adding benefit to benefit cannot become corruption; the medicine analogy is invalid because excess medicine is not truly beneficial, while religious benefit is always beneficial.

Checkpoint
How does the author respond to the claim that adding more benefit could cause corruption?
A) He agrees and says that is why there is a limit.
B) He says it is inconceivable that adding benefit to benefit would be corruption.
C) He says the medicine analogy is invalid because medicine is not beneficial.
D) He says religious benefit is limited.

Key Takeaway
The author maintains that religious benefit is not limited by material analogies, and that adding benefit to benefit always increases benefit, never corrupts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you ask God for something you believe is beneficial, do you assume that more of it would always be better? How does this passage challenge you to reconsider what truly counts as benefit in your faith?
?
Is there an area of your life where you have limited your devotion—such as prayer, charity, or seeking knowledge—because you fear it might become burdensome or harmful? What would it mean to trust that every increase in religious benefit is truly more beneficial?
?
The author argues that material analogies fail to capture religious benefit. In what specific way have you been relying on worldly logic to judge what God should give you? Will you repent of that and submit to God's measure of benefit?

The Seventh Objection: God's Knowledge and the Most Beneficial
Al-Ash'ari refutes al-Jubba'i's principle of the most beneficial using the case of foreknown disbelief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite principle of al-aslah and the logical objection raised against it regarding God's foreknowledge of disbelief.

Core Idea
What is truly best: opportunity for reward, or forced prevention of evil?

This passage presents a famous theological exchange between al-Ash'ari and al-Jubba'i over the Mu'tazilite principle that God must do what is most beneficial for His servants. The objection uses the case of someone God knows will disbelieve to challenge this principle. Follow the logical steps to see how al-Ash'ari forced al-Jubba'i to recant.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and first answer
The objection states that for someone foreknown to disbelieve, killing or maddening would be more beneficial, yet God does not do it. The opponent replies: burdening (taklīf) with exposure to bliss is more beneficial.
Notice the opponent's appeal to the higher station (aʿlā al-manzilatayn).
2
Examine al-Ash'ari's counter-argument
The author then asks: if burdening is best, why not apply it to a child who died? This is the subtle point (al-nuktah) that refutes al-Jubba'i.
This forced al-Jubba'i to recant his doctrine.
3
Consider the opponent's second answer
The opponent claims the child died because it was known he would mislead others, so his death was for the benefit of others.
4
Analyze the final refutation
The author asks: then why did God not cause Pharaoh, Haman, Mazdak, Zoroaster, and other great misleaders to die as children? Preventing benefit for an innocent for others' benefit would be foolish and unjust.
This shows the inconsistency of the opponent's position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السابع : من علم الله تعالى منه الكفر والعصيان أو الارتداد بعد الإسلام، فلا خفاء في أن الإماتة أو سلب العقل أصلح له ولم يفعل . فإن قيل : بل الأصلح التكليف والتعريض للنعيم الدائم لكونه أعلى المنزلتين. قلنا : فلم لم يفعل ذلك بمن مات طفلاً، وكيف لم يكن التكليف والتعريض لأعلى المنزلتين أصلح له ، وبهذه النكتة ألزم الأشعري الجبائي ورجع عن مذهبه. فإن قيل : علم من الطفل أنه إن عاش ضل وأضل غيره فأماته لمصلحة الغير. :قلنا فكيف لم يمت فرعون وهامان ومزدك وزرادشت وغيرهم من الضالين المضلين أطفالاً ، وكيف لم يكن منع الأصلح عمن لا جناية له لأجل مصلحة الغير سفهاً وظلماً .
The seventh: For one whom God Almighty knows will commit disbelief, disobedience, or apostasy after Islam, there is no concealment that causing death or removing intellect would be more beneficial for him, yet He does not do it. For it is said: Rather, the most beneficial is the burdening and exposure to eternal bliss because it is the higher of the two stations. We say: Then why did He not do that for the child who died? How was not the burdening and exposure to the higher station more beneficial for him? And by this subtle point al-Ash'ari forced al-Jubba'i, and he recanted his doctrine. For it is said: It was known about the child that if he lived he would go astray and mislead others, so He caused his death for the benefit of others. We say: Then why did He not cause Pharaoh, Haman, Mazdak, Zoroaster, and other misguiding misled ones to die as children? And how would the prevention of the most beneficial for one who has no crime, for the sake of benefit of others, not be foolishness and injustice?
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh objection and its answers. The objection: If God knows someone will disbelieve, then killing him or removing his intellect would be more beneficial, but God does not do it. The opponent replies: Burdening him (taklīf) and exposing him to eternal bliss is actually more beneficial because it is the higher station. The author refutes: Then why not burden the child who died? That would have been more beneficial for him. This is the famous argument by al-Ash'ari against al-Jubba'i. The opponent then objects: The child died because it was known he would mislead others. The author counters: Then why did God not cause Pharaoh, Haman, and other great misleaders to die as children? Preventing the most beneficial from an innocent person for the benefit of others would be foolish and unjust.

Key Terms
الكفر
العصيان
الارتداد
الإماتة
سلب العقل
التكليف
أعلى المنزلتين
النكتة
الأشعري
الجبائي
سفهاً
ظلماً

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection is a practical proposal to kill or make insane those who will disbelieve.
Correction: The objection is a logical reductio ad absurdum against the Mu'tazilite principle of al-aslah, not a prescription to harm people.

Checkpoint
What is the 'subtle point' (النكتة) that al-Ash'ari used to refute al-Jubba'i?
A) That God's knowledge is infinite.
B) That if burdening is best, then a child should have been burdened with adult responsibilities.
C) That God does not care about children.
D) That al-Jubba'i was a heretic.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari's refutation of al-Jubba'i using the child example demonstrates the incoherence of the Mu'tazilite principle of al-aslah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Mu'tazilite assumed they could determine what is 'most beneficial' for a person. When has your own assumption about what is best for you conflicted with what God actually decreed? Will you trust His wisdom even when you cannot see the benefit?
?
The child who died was spared future sin, yet the great misleaders were not. Think of a time when you were spared a sin or mistake by a circumstance beyond your control. Does that make you more grateful to God, or do you take it as a right? What do you owe in return?
?
If God knew every sin you would commit before you were born, and yet He still gave you life and the chance to repent, how does that reshape your view of His mercy? Are you using that chance, or assuming there will always be more time?

The Seventh Objection: Al-Aslah and Future Sin
The seventh objection showing inconsistency in applying al-aslah to those God knows will sin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seventh objection against the Mu'tazilite doctrine of al-aslah, showing that God's knowledge of future sin undermines the claim that He must always do what is most beneficial.

Core Idea
If al-aslah were obligatory, God would end the lives of future sinners early.

The Mu'tazila argue that God must always do what is most beneficial for His servants. But what about those whom God knows will become disbelievers or apostates? Would it not be more beneficial to prevent their eternal punishment by removing their life or intellect before they sin? In this brief but powerful objection, the author points out that God does not do this, thereby challenging the necessity of al-aslah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the English translation. Notice the conciseness of the objection.
2
Identify the scenario
The passage considers a person whom God knows will commit disbelief, disobedience, or apostasy after Islam. Understand why these are sins leading to eternal punishment.
God's foreknowledge does not cause sin but knows it certainly.
3
Understand the proposed beneficial action
The author suggests that death or removal of intellect would be 'more beneficial' (aṣlaḥ) for such a person, because it would prevent their sin and resulting punishment.
This follows Mu'tazilite reasoning: preventing harm is beneficial.
4
Recognize the contradiction
The objection points out that God does not actually do this for such individuals. This contradicts the Mu'tazilite claim that al-aslah is obligatory on God.
The argument is a reductio ad absurdum.
5
Reflect on the implication
Consider why the Mu'tazila cannot respond that God's action is still beneficial overall. The objection shows the principle is not universally applied.
This defends the Ash'ari view that God's actions are not bound by human notions of benefit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السابع : من علم الله تعالى منه الكفر والعصيان أو الارتداد بعد الإسلام، فلا خفاء في أن الإماتة أو سلب العقل أصلح له ولم يفعل.
The seventh: Whoever God knows from him disbelief, disobedience, or apostasy after Islam, there is no concealment that death or removal of intellect would be more beneficial for him, yet He did not do it.
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This is the seventh objection (السابع). The author argues that if the Mu'tazilite principle of al-aslah (the most beneficial) were truly obligatory on God, then for a person whom God knows will commit disbelief (kufr), disobedience ('isyān), or apostasy (irtidād) after embracing Islam, it would be 'more beneficial' (aṣlaḥ) for that person to die early or to be deprived of reason (salb al-'aql) so that they do not incur eternal punishment. However, God does not do that for many such individuals. This shows that God's actions are not bound by the Mu'tazilite notion of al-aslah, and that the Mu'tazila cannot claim that God must always do what is best for each individual servant.

Key Terms
الإماتة (death)
سلب العقل (removal of intellect)
أصلح (more beneficial)
الكفر والعصيان (disbelief and disobedience)
الارتداد (apostasy)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the objection suggests God should actually kill potential sinners or that God is unjust for not doing so.
Correction: The objection is purely logical: it shows the Mu'tazilite principle leads to an absurd conclusion, not that God should act that way. It defends the Ash'ari position that God's actions are not subject to external obligations.

Checkpoint
According to the seventh objection, what would be more beneficial for a person God knows will apostatize?
A) Letting him live to repent
B) Giving him more wealth and power
C) Death or removal of intellect
D) Causing him to forget Islam

Key Takeaway
The seventh objection uses God's foreknowledge of sin to argue that al-aslah cannot be obligatory, because God does not always do what is most beneficial for every individual.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
God knows every sin you will commit before you do it, yet He does not stop you. How does that truth confront your sense of personal responsibility right now?
?
If you truly believed that God could end your life at any moment to ‘save’ you from future sins, would you live differently today? What keeps you from that urgency?
?
The Mu'tazilite assumption that God must always do the most beneficial thing for you is false. Are you living as though you deserve only good from God? What does your expectation of ease reveal about your understanding of His wisdom?

The Eighth Argument: Supplication and the Flaw in Al-Aslah
Presents the eighth argument: supplication refutes al-aslah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the universal practice of supplication refutes the Mu'tazilite principle that God must always do what is most beneficial for His servants.

Core Idea
If God must always do the best, then prayer to change a situation becomes an absurd request for injustice.

The Mu'tazilites held that God is obligated to do what is most beneficial (al-aslah) for His servants. The eighth argument against this principle draws from the universal human practice of supplication (du'a). When people pray for relief from affliction, they are implicitly asking God to alter their situation. But if the original situation was already the most beneficial, such a prayer would demand that God abandon His obligation—making the prayer itself incoherent. This lesson unpacks that contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic sentence and its English translation to get the author's concise argument.
Notice how the premise 'all rational people pray' leads to the conclusion.
2
Identify the universal practice
Highlight the claim that all prophets, saints, and rational people agree on supplication to remove harm. This is the starting point of the argument.
3
Understand the Mu'tazilite commitment
Recall the Mu'tazilite principle of al-aslah: God must always do what is most beneficial. If that is true, then any prayer to change a situation is a request to alter what is already best.
This leads to the contradiction.
4
Follow the logical conclusion
The author says such a prayer would be asking God to 'change the most beneficial and prevent the obligatory, which is injustice.' This shows the absurdity of the Mu'tazilite view when applied to a universal practice.
The argument is a reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثامن : أجمع الأنبياء والأولياء وجميع العقلاء على الدعاء لدفع البلاء وكشف البأساء والضراء، فعندكم يكون ذلك سؤالاً من الله تعالى أن يغير الأصلح، ويمنع الواجب وهو ظلم.
The eighth: All the prophets, saints, and all rational people agree on supplication to repel affliction and remove distress and harm, so according to you that would be asking God Almighty to change what is most beneficial and to prevent the obligatory, which is injustice.
Source pages p. 334

Commentary
This passage is an argument against the Mu'tazilite principle of al-aslah (the most beneficial). It begins by establishing a universal practice: prophets, saints, and all rational people pray to God to remove affliction and harm. The author then points out the logical consequence of the Mu'tazilite view: if God were obligated to do the most beneficial for His servants, then a prayer asking Him to change a situation would be a request to alter what is already the most beneficial, implying that God is failing in His obligation. Thus, the practice of supplication contradicts the Mu'tazilite principle, which the author frames as an absurdity leading to injustice (zulm).

Key Terms
الأصلح
الدعاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that praying to God implies that God changes His mind or that prayer is useless if God's will is fixed.
Correction: The argument here does not address divine immutability; it specifically targets the Mu'tazilite claim that God is obligated to always do the most beneficial. The absurdity is that if that were true, prayer would be asking God to abandon His obligation.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the eighth argument?
A) Supplication is futile.
B) The Mu'tazilite principle of al-aslah leads to absurd consequences when applied to prayer.
C) Prayer is obligatory for prophets only.
D) God always answers prayer.

Key Takeaway
The universal practice of supplication shows that the Mu'tazilite obligation of al-aslah is untenable, because it would make every prayer for relief an incoherent request for injustice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a trial, do you truly believe that your supplication can change what God has decreed? Or do you secretly think that maybe God already chose the best, so prayer is just a formality? This argument shows that the universal practice of supplication implies God is not bound by an obligation to do the 'most beneficial' for you — He responds to your plea out of mercy, not duty. Will you now pray with the conviction that your request matters, or will you still hold back afraid that you are asking for something less than best?
?
Think of a specific hardship you are currently facing. Have you ever hesitated to pray for its removal because you thought, 'Maybe this is for my own good'? The source says that such thinking would make your prayer a request for injustice — because you would be asking God to stop doing what is obligatory (i.e., the best for you). Do you realize that by not praying earnestly, you are actually endorsing a view (al-aslah) that the author shows is false? What will you do with this realization today?
?
The prophets and saints are the highest examples of supplication. They prayed intensely to remove harm. If you are to follow their way, you must believe that your prayer is not an absurd request but a powerful act that God honors. Is there a sin, a broken relationship, or a personal weakness that you have been too proud or too hopeless to pray about? Will you now bring it before God, trusting that He is not a slave to a principle but the Master who hears and responds?

The Dilemma of al-Aslah: Prophet vs. Abu Jahl
Presents a dilemma against the Mu'tazilite principle of al-Aslah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the ninth argument against al-Aslah presents a dilemma that either strips divine favor from the Prophet or forces God into injustice.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazilite principle either makes the Prophet's merit his own doing or makes God unjust.

This passage presents a powerful dilemma against the Mu'tazilite principle of al-Aslah. It forces the Mu'tazilite to choose between two unacceptable outcomes: either the Prophet's superiority is reduced to his own choice, eliminating divine grace, or God fails to perform an obligation, implying injustice. This argument exposes a fatal flaw in the principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the dilemma carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the two horns of the dilemma. Notice the structure: 'If A then B, and if not A then C'.
Pay attention to the terms 'غاية مقدوره' and 'الإنعام والإحسان'.
2
Identify the two horns
Identify the first horn: giving Abu Jahl full benefits levels him with the Prophet, eliminating divine favor. The second horn: withholding some benefits violates the obligation, leading to foolishness and injustice.
3
Reflect on the theological implications
Consider why this dilemma is considered decisive. It shows the principle is self-defeating: it either negates divine grace (imtinān) or forces God to act unjustly, both of which are impossible for a just and generous God.
This connects to the broader debate on divine justice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التاسع : أن أعطى أبا جهل لعنه الله غاية مقدوره من المصالح والألطاف، فقد سوى بين النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم وبين أبي جهل في الإنعام والإحسان ورجع فضل النبي عليه السلام إلى محصن اختياره من غير امتنان وإن منع أبا جهل بعض المصالح والألطاف فقد ترك الواجب ولزم السفه والظلم على ما هو أصلكم الفاسد.
The ninth: If He gives Abu Jahl, may God curse him, the utmost of what is possible of benefits and favors, then He would have made equal between the Prophet, peace be upon him, and Abu Jahl in bestowal and beneficence, and the merit of the Prophet would be reduced to his own choice without any favor from God. And if He withholds from Abu Jahl some benefits and favors, then He has abandoned the obligatory, and foolishness and injustice follow, according to your corrupt principle.
Source pages p. 334

Commentary
This passage is the ninth argument against the Mu'tazilite principle. The author presents a dilemma: if God, according to al-aslah, must give each person the maximum possible benefits, then He would have to give the same benefits to both the Prophet and the arch-enemy Abu Jahl. This would eliminate any special divine favor (imtinān) for the Prophet, reducing his superiority to his own free choice (ikhtiyār). On the other hand, if God withholds some benefits from Abu Jahl, then He would be failing in His obligation, which would be foolishness (safah) and injustice (zulm) according to the Mu'tazilites' own standard. Thus, the principle is self-defeating.

Key Terms
أبو جهل
الإنعام
الامتنان
الاختيار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazilite principle ensures justice by requiring God to give each person the maximum benefit.
Correction: This argument shows that the principle either eliminates divine favor (by treating the Prophet and sinner equally) or forces God to act unjustly (by withholding benefits from the sinner), thus it is self-defeating.

Checkpoint
What does the ninth argument demonstrate about the Mu'tazilite principle?
A) It proves that Abu Jahl deserved punishment.
B) It leads to either leveling the Prophet with the disbeliever or obligating God to act unjustly.
C) It shows that God shows favoritism.
D) It confirms that all people receive equal benefits.

Key Takeaway
The ninth argument reveals that the Mu'tazilite principle of al-Aslah either negates divine favor or implies injustice, thus self-destructing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's favor is not the real source of your righteousness, then what is? Are you subtly relying on your own choices rather than on His grace, even while professing otherwise?
?
Do you ever feel that God owes you something—like a good outcome, or that He should treat you better than others? This dilemma shows that such thinking leads to absurdity. Will you surrender your expectations and trust His wisdom, even when His gifts seem unequal?
?
Consider the difference between the Prophet and Abu Jahl. If their ultimate states were purely from their own choices, then what is left of gratitude? What does this force you to acknowledge about the role of God's guidance in your own life?

The Tenth Argument: Divine Freedom and the Rejection of al-Aslah
Sanusi presents the final argument against al-Aslah: it negates divine freedom, citing Quranic verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazilite principle of al-aslah contradicts the doctrine of divine freedom and how the author refutes it using Quranic verses.

Core Idea
God's freedom to choose is incompatible with any obligation upon Him.

The tenth and final argument against al-Aslah strikes at the heart of divine freedom. If God were obligated to do the most beneficial for His servants, He would lose the freedom to bestow favors graciously. The author counters this by citing four Quranic verses that emphasize God's absolute choice in creation and favor. This lesson unpacks that argument and its scriptural basis.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping. Then read the English translation. Notice the flow: the problem, the Quranic proofs, and the strong conclusion.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the core problem
Identify why obligating al-aslah would remove room for God's gracious favor (tafaddul) and choice (khayrah). Understand the logical consequence the author highlights.
3
Examine the Quranic proofs
Locate the four Quranic verses cited. For each, ask: how does it show God's freedom to choose? Note the verbs 'chooses', 'singles out', 'gives', 'chose'.
These verses directly counter the idea of obligation.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Read the author's concluding remark: the corruptions of this principle are evident. Consider why the author is so emphatic. What does this say about the Mu'tazilite position?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العاشر: لو وجب الأصلح لما بقي للتفضل مجال، ولم يكن لله خيرة في الإنعام والافضال وهو باطل لقوله تعالى وربك يخلق ما يشاء ويختار يختص برحمته من يشاء يؤتي الحكمة من يشاء إن الله اصطفى آدم ونوحاً وآل ابراهيم وآل عمران على العالمين ولعمري إن مفاسد هذا الأصل أظهر من أن تخفى وأكثر من أن تحصى ولو وجب على الله الأصلح للعباد لما ضل المعتزلة طريق الرشاد.
The tenth: If the most beneficial were obligatory, there would be no room for gracious favor (tafaddul), and God would have no choice in bestowal and favor. That is false because of His saying: 'And your Lord creates what He wills and chooses' (28:68), 'He singles out for His mercy whom He wills' (2:105), 'He gives wisdom to whom He wills' (2:269), 'Indeed, God chose Adam and Noah and the family of Abraham and the family of Imran over the worlds' (3:33). By my life, the corruptions of this principle are more evident than to be hidden and more numerous than to be counted. If the most beneficial for servants were obligatory upon God, the Mu'tazila would not have strayed from the path of guidance.
Source pages p. 334

Commentary
This passage is the tenth and final argument against the Mu'tazilite principle. It focuses on divine freedom. If al-aslah were an obligation upon God, then there would be no room for tafaddul (gracious favor) and no khayrah (choice) for God in bestowing favors—He would be compelled. The author refutes this by citing four Quranic verses that emphasize God's absolute will and choice: He creates what He wills and chooses, singles out for mercy whom He wills, gives wisdom to whom He wills, and chose specific prophets over the worlds. These verses show that God freely elects and favors, contradicting any sense of obligation. The author then concludes with a strong remark: the corruptions of this principle are too obvious to hide and too numerous to count; if it were true, the Mu'tazila would not have gone astray.

Key Terms
التفضل
الخيرة
الأصلح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the principle of al-aslah exalts God's justice, but it actually limits His freedom and contradicts Quranic affirmations of His absolute choice.
Correction: The Quranic verses show God's freedom to choose and grant favors without obligation. Obligating God to do the most beneficial would negate the very concept of gracious favor and divine election.

Checkpoint
What is the main proof used in the tenth argument?
A) The consensus of scholars.
B) Quranic verses showing God freely chooses and favors.
C) The practice of the prophets.
D) Logical necessity.

Key Takeaway
Obligating God to do the most beneficial contradicts His freedom to choose and bestow favors graciously, as clearly shown in the Quran.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God were obliged to give you the best, would you still feel genuine gratitude for His favors, or would you feel entitled? How does this lesson confront your sense of entitlement before God?
?
Think of a blessing you consider a 'right'—health, provision, guidance. Based on this passage, are you willing to see it as a free gift (tafaddul) rather than something owed? What would change in your heart if you truly believed that?
?
The Mu'tazila strayed because they elevated a rational principle over the clear Quran. Are there any 'principles' in your own thinking that subtly challenge God's freedom to do as He wills in your life? Will you repent of those and submit to His absolute choice?

The Three Investigations of Divine Names
Introduces the new chapter on divine names, listing three topics to be discussed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three investigations that this chapter will explore regarding the names of Allah.

Core Idea
The Names of Allah are rooted in revelation, not human reasoning.

This brief passage opens Chapter Seven of the text, outlining the three key investigations to be discussed: the definition of 'name', the principle that Allah's names are tawqifiyya (based on revelation), and the meaning signified by each name. As we begin this chapter, these topics provide the framework for understanding the divine names.

Guided Path
1
Read the chapter heading
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Note that the author divides the chapter into three numbered investigations (mabāhith).
The list is concise; each item corresponds to a major discussion.
2
Understand 'tawqifiyya'
The second investigation states that Allah's names are tawqifiyya. This means they must be derived from revelation (Qur'an and authentic hadith), not from human reasoning. Reflect on why this is important.
This prevents speculation and ensures reverence.
3
Recognize the scope
The third investigation asks what each name signifies about Allah. This connects the linguistic concept of 'name' with theology. Understand that the chapter will explore these three dimensions.
The order is logical: definition, source, meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل السابع في أسماء الله تعالى وفيه مباحث : ١ - الاسم ٢ - أسماء الله تعالى توفيقية ٣ - في مدلول الاسم
Chapter Seven on the Names of Allah, and in it are investigations: 1 - The name. 2 - The names of Allah are tawqifiyya (based on revelation). 3 - On the meaning of the name.
Source pages p. 335

Commentary
This passage is the heading for a new chapter on the names of Allah. It lists three topics (mabāhith) to be covered: first, the definition of 'name' (al-ism) itself; second, the doctrine that the names of Allah are tawqifiyya, meaning they must be derived from revelation (Qur'an and Sunnah) and not from human reasoning; third, the signification or meaning (madlūl) of the name—what it indicates about Allah. This introduction sets the scope for the chapter's discussion.

Key Terms
الاسم
توفيقية
مدلول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Only names explicitly mentioned in the Qur'an are acceptable.
Correction: The term tawqifiyya includes names from authentic hadith as well, not only the Qur'an.

Checkpoint
What is the second investigation in this chapter?
A) The definition of the name.
B) The names of Allah are tawqifiyya.
C) The meaning of the name.
D) The attributes of Allah.

Key Takeaway
This introduction lays out the three investigations of the chapter: the nature of names, their revealed origin, and their signification.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever called Allah by a name or attribute that you assumed was appropriate without verifying it from the Quran or authentic Sunnah? What will you do now to ensure you only use revealed names?
?
This chapter teaches that the names of Allah are not just labels but have deep meanings (madlul). Which name of Allah do you understand the least, and what step will you take today to learn its meaning so your worship becomes more conscious?

Definition of al-Ism and the Necessary Distinction
Sanusi defines the three technical terms and explains the well-known view on name and named.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definitions of الاسم (name), المسمى (named), and التسمية (naming), and why their mutual distinction is necessary according to classical kalam.

Core Idea
Name, named, and naming are three distinct realities: the word, the meaning, and the act of referring.

Before delving into debates about Allah's names, we must first clarify basic terminology. The early scholars distinguished between the word used to refer (الاسم), the entity referred to (المسمى), and the act of referring itself (التسمية). This lesson establishes these definitions and explains why they must be kept distinct.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the three terms defined: الاسم, المسمى, التسمية.
2
Understand the necessary distinction
The author insists that these three are necessarily distinct. Reflect on why: a word is not the meaning, and the act of naming is a separate action.
3
Examine the well-known view
Consider the view that the name is identical to the named, with the example 'Zaid is a writer' versus 'Zaid is written'. This view actually interprets 'name' as the meaning.
This sets the stage for the later controversy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل السابع في أسمائه وفيه مباحث . معظم كلام القدماء في هذا الفصل شرح معاني أسماء الله ، ورجعها إلى ما له من الصفات والأفعال والمتأخرون فوضوا ذلك إلى ما صنف فيه من الكتب، واقتصروا على ما اختلفوا فيه من مغايرة الاسم للمسمى وكون أسماء الله تعالى توفيقية : قال المبحث الأول (الاسم) المبحث الأول الاسم (هو) اللفظ الموضوع، والمسمى هو المعنى الموضوع له، والتسمية وصفه أو ذكره فتغايرها ضروري وما اشتهر من أن الاسم نفس المسمى، والتسمية غيرهما، أريد بالاسم المدلول كما في قولنا زيد كاتب بخلاف قولنا زيد مكتوب وتفضل الشيخ بأن الاسم قد يكون نفس المسمى كقولنا الله - وقد يكون غيره كالخلق، وقد يكون بحيث لا هو ولا غيره كالعالم مبني على أنه أخذ المدلول بحيث التضمن، وأراد بالمسمى نفس الذات والحقيقة، وتمسك الفريقين بمثل قوله تعالى سبح اسم ربك الأعلى (۱) وقوله تعالى والله الأسماء الحسنى (٢). مع أنه يوهم أن المتنازع اسم وليس كذلك ضعيف، إذ قد يقدس الاسم، ويعبر بتعظيمه عن تعظيم الذات، وقد يراد به عند الشيخ التسمية، مع أن تعدد المفهومات لا ينافي وحدة الذات . (۲) فإن قيل : لا خفاء في تغاير اللفظ والمعنى وعدم تغاير المدلول والمسمى فلا يظهر ما يصلح محلاً للنزاع والاشتباه .
Chapter Seven on His Names, containing investigations. Most of the early scholars' discourse in this chapter is an explanation of the meanings of Allah's names, and they traced them back to His attributes and actions. The later scholars relegated that to the books composed on the subject, and limited themselves to what they differed over regarding the distinction of the name from the named and that the names of Allah are tawfiqiyya (derived from revelation). He said: The first investigation (al-ism): The first investigation: The name (al-ism) is the designated word (al-lafẓ al-mawḍūʿ); the named (al-musammā) is the meaning designated by it; and naming (al-tasmiya) is describing or mentioning it. Thus, their mutual distinction is necessary. And what is well-known—that the name (ism) is identical to the named (musammā), while naming (tasmiya) is other than the two—they intended by 'name' the meaning (al-madlūl), as in our saying 'Zaid is a writer' (Zaid kātib) as opposed to our saying 'Zaid is written' (Zaid maktūb). The Shaykh elaborated that the name may be identical to the named, like our saying 'Allah'; or it may be other than it, like 'creation'; or it may be such that it is neither it nor other than it, like 'the world'—based on his taking the meaning with respect to inclusion (taḍammun), and he intended by 'the named' the essence itself. And each of the two groups adduced evidence such as His saying, Exalted, 'Glorify the name of your Lord, the Most High' (Q. 87:1) and His saying, Exalted, 'And to Allah belong the beautiful names' (Q. 7:180). Yet, it is weak because it suggests that the disputed term is the name, but it is not so. For sometimes the name is sanctified, and its glorification is an expression of glorification of the essence, and sometimes by it (the name) the Shaykh intends naming (al-tasmiya). Moreover, multiplicity of concepts does not contradict the unity of the essence. (2) For if it is said: There is no hiddenness in the distinction between the word and the meaning, and there is no distinction between the meaning (madlūl) and the named (musammā); thus it is not apparent what could serve as a locus for dispute and confusion.
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage is from the chapter on the names of Allah. It first contrasts the approaches of early and later scholars: early scholars explained the meanings of the names in relation to attributes and actions, while later scholars focused on the issues of whether the name is different from the named and whether the names are tawfiqiyya. The author then begins the first investigation by defining three key terms: الاسم (the name) is the designated word, المسمى (the named) is the meaning designated by that word, and التسمية (naming) is the act of describing or mentioning. He emphasizes that these three are necessarily distinct. He then addresses a well-known view that الاسم is identical to المسمى, while التسمية is different. According to this view, by 'name' they actually mean the meaning (المدلول), illustrated by the example 'Zaid is a writer' where the word 'Zaid' refers to the person, versus 'Zaid is written' where 'Zaid' refers to the written word. The Shaykh (perhaps Ibn Sīnā or another authority) elaborates that الاسم can be: (1) identical to المسمى, like 'Allah'; (2) different, like 'creation'; or (3) neither identical nor different, like 'the world'. This is based on considering the meaning in terms of inclusion (التضمن), where المسمى is the essence itself. Both sides cite Quranic verses such as 'Glorify the name of your Lord' and 'To Allah belong the beautiful names'. The author critiques this as weak because it wrongly suggests the dispute is about the name as such, when in fact the name may be sanctified as a way to glorify the essence, and the Shaykh may intend by 'name' the act of naming (التسمية). He concludes that multiplicity of concepts does not contradict the unity of the essence. Finally, an objection is raised: since the distinction between word and meaning is obvious, and there is no distinction between the meaning (المدلول) and the named (المسمى), there is no basis for the dispute. The answer to this objection is not provided in the available text.

Key Terms
الاسم
المسمى
التسمية
المدلول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the definition of الاسم (the name)?
A) The meaning designated by the word
B) The designated word
C) The act of naming
D) The named entity

Key Takeaway
The name (الاسم) is the word used to refer; the named (المسمى) is the meaning; naming (التسمية) is the act of referring; they are necessarily distinct.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call upon Allah by His names, do you realize that the name is not the same as the Named? How does this distinction affect your awareness of the One you are addressing? Will you begin to consciously differentiate between the word and the Being when you recite His names?
?
In your daily speech, you use many names. Have you ever confused the name with the thing itself? This lesson teaches that the name and named are distinct. How can you apply this insight to avoid idolizing words or concepts, keeping your focus on the reality they point to?
?
The well-known view that 'the name is the named' is clarified here. Do you hold any such sayings without understanding their intended meaning? Will you commit to seeking the true meaning behind theological statements rather than taking them literally?

The Shaykh's Classification of Name-Named Relations and the Objection
Sanusi presents the Shaykh's threefold classification, critiques the evidence, and ends with an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three types of relations between name and named as elaborated by the Shaykh, the weak evidence cited by both sides, and the objection raised against the very basis of the dispute.

Core Idea
A name can be identical, different, or neither—but the real dispute may be misplaced.

The Shaykh adds nuance to the discussion by proposing three possible relations between the name and the named: identity, difference, or a middle state. He supports this with Quranic verses, but the author criticizes the evidence as weak. Finally, an objection questions whether there is any real locus for dispute at all, given the clear distinction between word and meaning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Shaykh's three categories
Read the passage where the Shaykh states that the name may be identical to the named (like 'Allah'), different (like 'creation'), or neither (like 'the world'). Understand what each means.
The classification is based on the concept of inclusion (التضمن).
2
Examine the Quranic evidence
Both sides cite verses like 'Glorify the name of your Lord' and 'To Allah belong the beautiful names'. The author critiques this as weak, explaining that glorifying the name can be a way to glorify the essence.
3
Understand the author's critique
The author points out that the name may be sanctified as an expression of glorifying the essence, and that the Shaykh sometimes uses 'name' to mean naming. Also, multiplicity of concepts does not contradict unity of essence.
4
Consider the objection
The objection states that since the distinction between word and meaning is obvious, and there is no distinction between meaning and named, there is no genuine dispute.
The answer to this objection is not given in the available text.

Misconception: The debate over name and named is about whether the word 'Allah' is the same as the Essence.

?

Early and Later Scholars on Allah's Names
Contrasting early and later scholars' approaches to Allah's names.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between the early and later scholars' methods in studying Allah's names, and the two central issues they debated.

Core Idea
Scholars shifted from explaining names to debating their essence and source.

The study of Allah's names has a rich history in Islamic theology. The earliest scholars focused on explaining the meanings of the names by linking them to attributes and actions. Later scholars, however, narrowed their focus to two key disputed questions: whether the name is distinct from the named, and whether names come only from revelation. This lesson introduces that shift and sets the stage for the detailed discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get a sense of the contrast between early and later scholars.
2
Identify the two groups
Notice that the passage divides scholars into early (qudama') and later (muta'akhkhirun) and describes their different approaches.
3
Recognize the disputed issues
The later scholars confined themselves to two disagreements: the relation between name and named, and the tawfiqiyya nature of names. Ask yourself why these became central.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
معظم كلام القدماء في هذا الفصل شرح معاني أسماء الله، ورجعها إلى ما له من الصفات والأفعال والمتأخرون فوضوا ذلك إلى ما صنف فيه من الكتب، واقتصروا على ما اختلفوا فيه من مغايرة الاسم للمسمى وكون أسماء الله تعالى توفيقية.
Most of the discourse of the early scholars in this chapter is an explanation of the meanings of Allah's names, and they traced them back to His attributes and actions. The later scholars delegated that to what has been compiled in books on the subject, and confined themselves to what they disagreed upon concerning the distinction between the name and the named, and that Allah's names are tawfiqiyyah (determined by revelation).
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
This passage is an introductory statement that contrasts the approach of early and later scholastic theologians (mutakallimun) regarding the study of Allah's names. The early scholars focused on explaining the meanings of the names by referring them to divine attributes and actions. The later scholars, however, did not treat the subject in depth in this work but referred to specialized books. They limited their discussion to two contested issues: whether the name is identical to or different from the named (musamma), and whether Allah's names are tawfiqiyyah (i.e., only to be derived from revelation, not from human reasoning).

Key Terms

القدماء
المتأخرون
مغايرة الاسم للمسمى
توفيقية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Later scholars completely abandoned the study of Allah's names.
Correction: They delegated the detailed explanation to specialized books and focused only on specific disputed issues.

Checkpoint

A) A full explanation of the meanings of all names.
B) Confining themselves to disputed issues: the distinction between name and named, and whether names are tawfiqiyyah.
C) A complete refutation of the early scholars' views.
D) A list of all names and their derivations from actions and attributes.

Key Takeaway
The early scholars explained names by linking to attributes and actions, while later scholars focused on two debated issues: the identity of name and named, and the revealed nature of names.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you learn a new name of Allah, do you stop at memorizing it, or do you actively connect it to His actions in your life? This week, choose one name and journal how you have seen that attribute manifested in your day.
?
How often do you describe Allah using terms not found in the Quran or Sunnah? In your own conversations about God, are you careful to only attribute to Him what He has revealed about Himself? Pledge to check your descriptions against revelation.
?
Early scholars linked Allah's names to His attributes and actions. Think of the name 'Ar-Rahman' — what specific act of mercy did you experience today? If you cannot remember, what does that say about your mindfulness of His names in daily life?
